Preference of Isti'naf over Bina' to Avoid Shubhat al-Khilaf
Explaining why isti'naf is afdal and the nuanced applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on isti'naf and bina' after hadath during salah, and apply the nuanced preference to different scenarios (munfarid, imam, muqtadi).

Core Idea
Isti'naf is better than bina' to avoid the shadow of disagreement, but the ruling varies by role.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position on what to do when a person experiences hadath during prayer. While both restarting (isti'naf) and continuing from where you left off (bina') are permissible, the school advises that starting over is better—especially for an individual. However, for an imam and follower, building on the existing prayer is recommended to preserve the virtue of congregation, unless another congregation is available. This nuanced ruling reflects the Hanafi commitment to both legal validity and practical unity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the ruling on isti'naf vs bina'? The text states that isti'naf is afdal (better) than bina' to avoid shubhat al-khilaf.
Both are permissible, but preference is given to isti'naf.
2
Understand the basis of preference
Why is isti'naf afdal? Because it avoids shubhat al-khilaf (shadow of disagreement), meaning it prevents any resemblance to positions that oppose the madhhab.
This is a taqwa-based preference, not a legal obligation.
3
Note the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
The preference varies: for the munfarid (individual), isti'naf is afdal; for the imam and muqtadi (follower), bina' is afdal to preserve the virtue of congregation. Some scholars add that if another congregation can be formed, then imam and muqtadi should also do isti'naf.
This shows how the madhhab balances individual preference with communal benefit.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The operative ruling: isti'naf is afdal for the munfarid; for the imam and muqtadi, bina' is afdal unless another congregation is available. This is the position relied upon in fatwa.
The text does not provide a definitive tarjih, but later mashayikh have settled on this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبلوى فيما يسبق الخ سے امام شافعی کے قیاس کا جواب ہے جواب کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ حدث سابق یعنی غیر اختیاری حدث حدث عمد پر قیاس کرنا درست نہیں کیونکہ ان دونوں کے درمیان فرق موجود ہے۔ اس لئے کہ غیر اختیاری حدث میں ابتلا ء ہے کیونکہ وہ نی اس کے فعل کے حاصل ہوتا ہے لہذا اس کو معذور قرار دینا جائز ہوگا۔ اس کے بر خلاف حدث عمد کہ اس میں یہ بات نہیں ہے پس اس آن کے ہوتے ہوئے قیاس کرنا کس طرح درست ہو گا۔
والاستيناف افضل تحرزا عن شبهة الخلاف وقيل المنفرد يستقبل والامام والمقتدى يبنى صيانة لفضيل الجماعة
ترجمہ اور از سرنو پڑھنا افضل ہے تا کہ اختلاف کے شبہ سے احتراز ہو جائے۔ اور کہا گیا کہ منفرد استیناف کرے اور امام اور مقتدی کریں تا کہ جماعت کی فضیلت محفوظ رہے۔
تشریح صاحب قدوری نے کہا کہ مسئلہ مذکور میں اگر چہ بنا، کرنا جائز ہے لیکن از سر نو پڑھنا افضل ہے تا کہ شبہ خلاف سے احترام جائے ۔ اور اگر کوئی یہ کہے کہ استیناف کے اندر ابطال عمل ہے تو ہم جواب دیں گے کہ بلاشبہ ابطال عمل ہے مگر اکمال کے لئے اور کیوں ابطلال عمل محمود ہے نہ کہ مذموم بعض مشائخ نے کہا کہ منفرد کو نئے سرے سے پڑھنا افضل ہے اور امام اور مقتدی کو بنا کر نا افضل ہے ۔ جماعت کی افضلیت محفوظ رہے اور بعض حضرات نے کہا کہ اگر امام اور مقتدی کو دوسری جماعت مل سکتی ہو تو استیناف افضل ہے اور اگر نیا سکتی ہو تو بنا ، افضل ہے۔
And from 'al-balwa fima yasbuq' etc. is the answer to Imam Shafi'i's qiyas. The gist of the answer is that it is not correct to analogize a prior hadath (unintentional hadath) to intentional hadath because there is a difference between the two. Because in unintentional hadath there is affliction (bala') since it occurs without his action, therefore it is permissible to consider him excused. In contrast, intentional hadath does not have this. So how can qiyas be correct while this is present? And isti'naf (starting anew) is better (afdal) to avoid the shadow of disagreement (shubhat al-khilaf). And it was said: the individual (munfarid) should start anew, while the imam and follower (muqtadi) should build (yabni) to preserve the virtue of congregation. Translation: And starting anew is better so that the shadow of disagreement is avoided. And it was said: the individual should start anew, and the imam and follower build to preserve the virtue of congregation. Commentary: Sahib al-Quduri said: In the mentioned issue, although building (bina') is permissible, starting anew is better to avoid the shadow of disagreement. And if someone says that in isti'naf there is nullification of action (ibtal 'amal), we will answer: undoubtedly there is nullification of action, but it is for completion, and such nullification is praiseworthy, not blameworthy. Some mashayikh said: For the individual, starting anew is afdal; for the imam and follower, building (bina') is afdal to preserve the virtue of congregation. And some said: If the imam and follower can find another congregation, then isti'naf is afdal; if not, then bina' is afdal.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the preference of isti'naf (starting the prayer anew) over bina' (building upon the existing prayer) when a person experiences hadath (ritual impurity) during salah. The author first responds to Imam al-Shafi'i's qiyas (analogy) that compares unintentional hadath to intentional hadath. The Hanafi position insists that they are fundamentally different because unintentional hadath involves an affliction (bala') and the person is excused, whereas intentional hadath is a deliberate act. Thus, the analogy is invalid. The core ruling is that isti'naf is afdal (better) to avoid the shadow of disagreement (shubhat al-khilaf). However, there are nuanced opinions: some say the munfarid (individual praying alone) should perform isti'naf, while the imam and muqtadi (follower) should perform bina' to preserve the virtue of congregation. Others qualify that if the imam and muqtadi can find another congregation, isti'naf is better; otherwise, bina' is better. The commentary by Sahib al-Quduri emphasizes that even though bina' is permissible, isti'naf is preferable to avoid even the appearance of violating the consensus. The practice of the Khulafa' al-Rashidin and the Sahabah is cited as proof for the permissibility of bina', but the preference for isti'naf is a later fatwa to avoid any resemblance to opposing views.

Key Terms
استيناف (isti'naf): Starting the prayer completely anew from the beginning after hadath occurs.
بناء (bina'): Continuing the prayer from where one left off after performing wudu, without restarting.
شبهة الخلاف (shubhat al-khilaf): The shadow of disagreement; the possibility that one's practice might resemble a view opposed to the majority or the preferred view, even if one's own view is valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the preferred action for a munfarid (individual praying alone) who experiences hadath during salah?
A) He must perform bina' (continue after wudu).
B) He should perform isti'naf (restart) because it is afdal.
C) He may choose either, but bina' is better.
D) His prayer is invalid; he must repeat it after the time.

Key Takeaway
Isti'naf is recommended over bina' for the individual, but for the imam and muqtadi, bina' is preferred to preserve congregational virtue unless another congregation is available.

Refuting the Shafi'i Analogy: Unintentional vs Intentional Hadath
Responding to Imam Shafi'i's qiyas by distinguishing unintentional from intentional hadath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects the Shafi'i qiyas that analogizes unintentional hadath to intentional hadath, and identify the specific difference that invalidates the analogy.

Core Idea
Unintentional hadath involves affliction (bala'), so the person is excused, unlike intentional hadath, making qiyas invalid.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi response to a key objection from the Shafi'i school. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that if bina' is allowed for unintentional hadath, it should also be allowed for intentional hadath by way of qiyas (analogy). The Hanafi school rejects this analogy on the grounds that the two cases are fundamentally different. The difference lies in the presence of affliction (bala') in unintentional hadath, which renders the person excused, whereas intentional hadath is a deliberate act with no excuse. Understanding this distinction is crucial for appreciating how Hanafi usul applies the principle of illa (effective cause) in qiyas.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i position and their qiyas
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that if bina' is permissible after unintentional hadath, it should also be permissible after intentional hadath, because both involve leaving the prayer and returning. This is a qiyas based on the perceived similarity.
The Shafi'i view is that bina' is invalid altogether, so they use this analogy to show inconsistency in the Hanafi position.
2
Identify the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school rejects the qiyas by pointing out a crucial difference: unintentional hadath involves affliction (bala') and the person is excused (ma'dhur). Intentional hadath does not.
The existence of bala' changes the legal ruling.
3
Understand the concept of bala' and 'udhr
Bala' means the person is tested with something beyond their control. Therefore, the law treats them with leniency. This is absent in intentional acts.
This is a key principle: necessity or affliction can alter rulings.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle of illa
For qiyas to be valid, the illa (effective cause) must be common to both cases. Here, the illa in unintentional hadath includes 'udhr (excuse), which is missing in intentional hadath. Therefore, the analogy fails.
This demonstrates the Hanafi insistence on precise tahrir al-manat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in the text, why is the Shafi'i qiyas between unintentional and intentional hadath invalid?
A) Because the hadith used by Shafi'i is weak.
B) Because unintentional hadath involves bala' (affliction) and the person is excused, but intentional hadath does not.
C) Because the Hanafi school does not use qiyas as a source of law.
D) Because the Shafi'i school does not accept the validity of bina' at all.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects the Shafi'i analogy by distinguishing unintentional hadath (with 'udhr) from intentional hadath (without 'udhr), preserving the consistency of their ruling on bina'.

Mutayammim Seeing Water Invalidates Prayer
Ruling on mutayammim who sees water during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a mutayammim sees water during prayer and explain the basis for invalidation.

Core Idea
Seeing water during prayer removes the basis for tayammum, making prayer invalid immediately.

This lesson addresses a specific rule regarding tayammum in prayer. The text from Hidayāh states a clear ruling: if a person performing tayammum sees water while still in prayer, the prayer becomes invalid. This highlights the conditional nature of tayammum as a substitute for water; once water becomes available, the substitute is no longer valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the event
The act is prayer performed with tayammum. The event is seeing water during that prayer. Recognize that the ruling hinges on the timing of seeing water—during the prayer, not before or after.
2
State the ruling
The text says the prayer becomes invalid (bāṭil). Memorize the exact phrase: 'فان رأى المتيمم الماء في صلاته بطلت'.
3
Explain the legal reason (ʿillah)
Tayammum is permitted only when water is absent or unusable. Seeing water during prayer indicates that the condition for tayammum is no longer met, so the prayer's purity basis is broken.
In Hanafi fiqh, tayammum is a substitute (badal) that ends when the original (water) becomes available.
4
Apply the ruling to a contrasting scenario
Consider what happens if water is seen after the prayer is completed. The prayer remains valid because the condition was satisfied for the entire prayer. This highlights the critical timing.
This distinction is important for proper application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان رأى المتيمم الماء في صلاته بطلت وقد مر من قبل ترجمہ پس اگر قیم نے اپنی نماز میں پانی دیکھا تو اس کی نماز باطل ہوگئی۔ اور یہ مسئلہ پہلے گذر چکا ہے۔ کرنے کی
So if the mutayammim sees water during his prayer, his prayer becomes invalid. And this issue has passed before.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This is a ruling statement: if a person who is performing tayammum (dry ablution due to lack of water or inability to use it) sees water while still in prayer, his prayer becomes invalid (bāṭil). The reason is that the basis for tayammum (lack of water) is no longer present, so the purity condition is broken. The text notes that this ruling was mentioned earlier, implying it is established and well-known in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
متيمم (mutayammim): A person who performs tayammum (dry ablution with earth) due to unavailability of water or inability to use it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person performing tayammum sees water while still in prayer, what happens to the prayer?
A) The prayer remains valid because it was started with tayammum.
B) The prayer becomes invalid because the condition for tayammum is lost.
C) He must continue the prayer and then repeat it later.
D) He can make a quick wudu and continue the prayer.

Key Takeaway
The presence of water during a tayammum prayer invalidates the prayer immediately because the basis for the substitute purity is removed.

Definition of Kalam al-Nas Invalidating Prayer
Defines kalam al-nas and discusses condition of meaningful letters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of kalam al-nas and apply its two conditions (two meaningful letters) to determine when prayer is invalidated.

Core Idea
Human speech in prayer invalidates only if it is two meaningful letters — a single letter never does.

What exactly is 'human speech' (kalam al-nas) that voids the prayer? The Hanafi school gives a precise definition: it must be meaningful and consist of at least two alphabetic letters. Even if all letters are 'extra' (huruf zawa'id), if they form a meaningful construction, it counts. This lesson unpacks that definition and examines a nuanced disagreement within the school about the minimum number of extra letters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage defines what speech (kalam) invalidates prayer. Identify that the act is speaking during prayer and the ruling is that it invalidates (mufsid) under specific conditions.
2
Extract the two conditions
From the text, list the two necessary conditions: (1) the utterance consists of huruf al-hija (alphabetic letters), and (2) those letters are meaningful (mufid ma'na). A single letter is excluded by consensus.
3
Analyze the 'extra letters' nuance
The passage argues that speech composed entirely of extra letters (huruf zawa'id) can still be kalam if it has a grammatical structure (e.g., mubtada+ khabar). Note the example 'antum al-yawma salamtumuni-ha'.
4
Contrast the intra-Hanafi difference
Imam Abu Yusuf held that exactly two extra letters do not invalidate, while more than two do. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) say any meaningful two-letter speech invalidates regardless of letter type. Compare the positions.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is the majority view: any meaningful speech of two or more letters invalidates prayer. Abu Yusuf's exception for two extra letters is not considered strong.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يفسد الصلوة وما يكره فيها
وهذا لا يقوى الج سے کہتے ہیں جو اصول امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک بیان فرمالیا ہے۔ وہ قوی نہیں ہے کیونکہ مفسد نماز کلام الناس ہے اور عرف عام میں کلام الناس ہونا دو باتوں کے تابع ہے: یہ کہ حروف ہجاء پائے جائیں حتی کہ اگر مصلی کی آواز میں کوئی حرف ہی و بالاتفاق مفسد نہیں ہے، دوم یہ کہ وہ حروف ہجا مفید معنی ہوں حتی کہ اگر وہ حروف مفید معنی نہ ہوں تو مفسد نماز نہ ہوگا۔ اور یہ بات مسلم ہے کہ کلام ہوتا اس وقت بھی متحقق ہو جاتا ہے جب کہ اس کے تمام حروف زوائد میں سے ہوں مثلا کسی نے کہا کہ انتم اليوم سالتمونيھا، اس جملہ میں مبتداء و خبر کی ترکیب ہے اور اس کلام کے تمام حروف زوائد میں سے ہیں اس کے باوجود مفسد نماز ہے۔ پس معلوم ہوا کہ مطلقاً کلام مفسد نماز ہے حروف زوائد پر مشتمل ہو یا حروف اصلی پر۔ مگر صاحب نہایہ نے جواب میں فرمایا کہ امام ابو ہونے کا کام د حرفوں میں ہے یعنی اگر کام دو حرف زائد پر مشتمل ہو تو وہ مفسد نماز نہیں ہوگا اور اگر دو حروف سے زائد حروف پر مشتمل ہو سب حروف زوائد میں سے ہوں تو امام ابو یوسف کا قول بھی طرفین کے قول کے مانند ہے یعنی نماز فاسد ہو جائے گی۔
Chapter on what invalidates prayer and what is disliked in it. And this is not strong... they say what Imam Abu Yusuf has stated according to his principles. It is not strong because that which invalidates prayer is kalam al-nas, and in common custom, being kalam al-nas depends on two things: first, that alphabetic letters are found, to the extent that if the praying person's sound contains [even] one letter, it does not invalidate by consensus; second, that those alphabetic letters convey meaning, such that if they do not convey meaning, it does not invalidate prayer. And it is established that speech is also realized when all its letters are from the extra letters, for example if someone says 'antum al-yawma salamtumuni-ha', in this sentence there is the structure of mubtada and khabar, and all its letters are from the extra letters, yet it invalidates prayer. So it is known that absolutely speech invalidates prayer, whether consisting of extra letters or original letters. But the author of al-Nihayah responded that Imam Abu Yusuf's view is regarding two letters: that is, if the speech consists of two extra letters, it does not invalidate prayer; but if it consists of more than two letters, even if all are from extra letters, then Imam Abu Yusuf's opinion is like that of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) i.e. the prayer becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a definition of and ruling on kalam al-nas (human speech) that invalidates prayer. It begins by addressing a previous argument regarding Imam Abu Yusuf's view. The author explains that the ruling on invalidation depends on two conditions: (1) the utterance consists of alphabetic letters (huruf al-hija), and (2) those letters convey meaning (mufid ma'na). A single letter, even if meaningful, does not invalidate by consensus. A string of letters that are all 'extra' (huruf zawa'id) can still be considered kalam as long as it conveys a structured meaning, as in the example 'antum al-yawma salamtumuni-ha' which has a mubtada-khabar structure. This shows that kalam al-nas is absolute regardless of letter type. The passage then notes a nuance from Sahib al-Nihayah regarding Imam Abu Yusuf: if the utterance consists of exactly two extra letters, it does not invalidate; if more than two, it does, aligning with the Sahibayn. This is an intra-Hanafi difference on the threshold of letter count.

Key Terms
كلام الناس (kalam al-nas): Human speech that is not Quran, dhikr, or du'a; it invalidates prayer if meaningful and consisting of two or more letters.
حروف هجاء (huruf al-hija): The Arabic alphabetic letters that form words.
حروف زوائد (huruf zawa'id): Letters that are not part of the root (original) letters of a word, e.g. alif, waw, ya in certain conjugations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One letter
B) Two letters
C) Three letters
D) Any number of extra letters

Key Takeaway
Kalam al-nas that invalidates prayer in the Hanafi school is defined as meaningful speech consisting of at least two alphabetic letters, with the majority view overriding Abu Yusuf's exception for two extra letters.

Ruling on Coughing, Sneezing, and Belching in Prayer
States the ruling on coughing with or without excuse, and analogizes sneezing and belching

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on involuntary sounds in prayer and distinguish between excused and unexcused coughing.

Core Idea
In prayer, voluntary speech invalidates; involuntary bodily sounds are excused even if letters appear.

Bodily sounds like coughing, sneezing, and belching can happen during prayer, but not all of them invalidate it. The Hanafi school makes a key distinction: whether the sound was voluntary (intentional) or involuntary (unavoidable). In this lesson, we examine the ruling for coughing with or without an excuse, and how sneezing and belching are analogized.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is coughing (tanahnuh) that produces letters. The ruling depends on whether it is with or without excuse.
2
Determine the condition (excuse)
If the cough is voluntary (without excuse) and letters are produced, the prayer is invalidated. If it is due to an excuse (e.g., dust, choking), it is excused and valid.
The excuse must be genuine and unavoidable.
3
Apply the analogy to sneezing and belching
Sneezing and belching are always considered involuntary, so they are excused even if letters appear, just like coughing with an excuse.
This shows the underlying principle: involuntary sounds are not kalam al-nas.
4
Note the source and authority
The ruling is from the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) as presented in the text. Abu Hanifa's view is not mentioned here, but the relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn.
Cross-madhhab: Shafi'is hold that any sound with letters invalidates, even if involuntary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وان تنحنح بغير عذر بان لم يكن مدفوعا اليه وحصل به الحروف ينبغى ان يفسد عندهما وان كان بعذر الهر عفو كالعطاس والجشاء اذا حصل به حروف
ترجمہ اور اگر مصلی نے شیخ کیا بغیر عذر کے بائیں طور کو مدفوع الیہ نہ ہوا اور ا سے حروف پیدا ہو جائیں تو مناسب یہ ہے کہ طرفین کے نزدیک نماز فاسد ہو جائے اور سح عذر کی وجہ سے ہو تو یہ معاف ہے جیسے چھینک اور ڈکار جب کہ اس سے حروف پیدا ہو جائیں ۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر مصلی نے شیخ کیا یعنی کھنکھارا اور اس کی وجہ سے حروف بھی پیدا ہو گئے مثلات) بالفتح یا بانضم ) کہا تو اس کی صورتیں ہیں عذر کی وجہ سے ہوگا یا بغیر عذر کے۔ اگر بغیر عذر کے ہو یعنی اضطراری نہ ہو بلکہ اختیاری ہوتو طرفین کے نزدیک نماز فاسد : ہاں چاہنے اور اگر عذر کی وجہ سے ہے تو یہ معاف ہے یعنی نماز فاسد نہ ہوگی جیسے چھینک اور ڈکار سے نماز فاسد نہیں ہوتی اگر چہ اس سے حروف ظاہر ہو جائیں۔
What is the ruling on coughing in prayer, whether due to an excuse or without an excuse, and similarly sneezing and belching? [Arabic:] And if he coughs without an excuse, such that he was not compelled to do so and letters are produced, it is appropriate that according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it invalidates. But if it is due to an excuse, it is excused, like sneezing and belching when letters are produced. [Urdu translation:] If the praying person coughed without an excuse... then according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) the prayer becomes invalid. And if it is due to an excuse, it is excused, like sneezing and belching when letters are produced. Explanation: If the praying person coughed and letters were produced, like saying 'ah' or 'uh', then there are two cases: with an excuse or without. If without excuse (voluntary), then by the two the prayer is invalid; if with an excuse, it is excused, just as sneezing and belching do not invalidate even if letters appear.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on involuntary bodily sounds in prayer. The key distinction is whether the sound was intentional (without excuse) or involuntary (with excuse). According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if a person coughs without being forced (i.e., voluntarily) and letters are produced, the prayer is invalidated because it constitutes kalam. If the cough is due to an excuse (e.g., dust or choking), it is excused and does not invalidate. The same applies to sneezing and belching: even if letters appear, they are excused due to their involuntary nature. This shows that the Hanafi school distinguishes between voluntary and involuntary speech, and that letters produced involuntarily do not count as kalam al-nas.

Key Terms
تنحنح (tanahnuh): Clearing the throat or coughing, which may produce audible letters.
عذر ('udhr): An unavoidable reason, like dust or a choking sensation, that forces the sound.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Prayer is valid and excused
C) Prayer is valid but requires sujood al-sahw
D) Prayer is invalid unless the person spits

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, involuntary bodily sounds with letters (like coughing with excuse, sneezing, belching) are excused and do not invalidate prayer; only voluntary speech invalidates.

Responding to a Sneeze Invalidates Prayer
Ruling that saying yarhamuka Allah invalidates prayer; 'alhamdulillah' does not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on responding to a sneeze in prayer and distinguish between invalidating ('yarhamuka Allah') and non-invalidating ('alhamdulillah') responses.

Core Idea
Saying 'yarhamuka Allah' invalidates prayer; 'alhamdulillah' does not because it is dhikr, not kalam al-nas.

This lesson examines a specific act that may invalidate prayer: responding to a sneeze. The Hanafi school distinguishes between customary speech (kalam al-nas), which nullifies prayer, and dhikr, which does not. Understanding this distinction helps preserve the validity of salah while maintaining proper etiquette.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Read the passage to see what act is being ruled upon (responding to a sneeze) and what the ruling is (invalidates prayer when saying 'yarhamuka Allah').
The ruling is specific to the responder, not the sneezer.
2
Determine the basis for invalidation
Understand why 'yarhamuka Allah' invalidates: it is commonly used in addressing people, so it falls under kalam al-nas.
Kalam al-nas is any speech that is not remembrance of Allah.
3
Distinguish the exception
Note that 'alhamdulillah' does not invalidate because it is not a customary response to a sneeze; it is considered dhikr.
The mashayikh relied on custom ('urf) to make this distinction.
4
Consider intra-Hanafi variant
Examine the narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that moving the tongue while saying 'alhamdulillah' invalidates; otherwise it is valid.
This shows the importance of intention and action.
5
Apply to a scenario
Test your understanding by applying the ruling to a new case, e.g., saying 'Allahumma barik' to a sneezer.
Consider whether the phrase is customary or dhikr.

Responding to a sneeze in prayer invalidates prayer. [Arabic:] If someone sneezes and another person who is in prayer says 'yarhamuka Allah', his prayer becomes invalid because it is commonly used in addressing people, so it is from their speech. Unlike the case when the sneezer or the hearer says 'alhamdulillah', according to what the mashayikh said, because saying 'alhamdulillah' is not customary as a response. [Urdu explanation:]... The explanation: A person sneezes, then another person who is in prayer says 'yarhamuka Allah', so the speaker's prayer becomes invalid because 'yarhamuka Allah' contains the letter kaaf of address and it is common usage among people. Thus it is from kalam al-nas. Conversely, if the sneezing person or the hearer who is praying says 'alhamdulillah', according to the mashayikh it does not invalidate because saying 'alhamdulillah' is not considered a response in custom, rather it is dhikr of Allah, and dhikr is part of prayer... The author of al-`Inayah transmitted from al-Muhit that there is a narration from Imam Abu Hanifah that the sneezing person should say 'alhamdulillah' in his heart without moving his tongue; if he moves his tongue, his prayer becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling on responding to a sneeze while in prayer. The act of saying 'yarhamuka Allah' to a sneezer invalidates the prayer of the responder, because it is a conventional response in human conversation and thus falls under kalam al-nas. However, if the praying person says 'alhamdulillah' in response to his own or another's sneeze, it does not invalidate, according to the mashayikh, because 'alhamdulillah' is not customary as a response but rather dhikr. The passage also quotes a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that one should say 'alhamdulillah' silently in the heart without moving the tongue; moving the tongue would invalidate the prayer. This shows the Hanafi concern for preserving the prayer's integrity while allowing for appropriate dhikr.

Key Terms
تشميت (tashmit): Responding to a sneezer by saying 'yarhamuka Allah'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Prayer is invalid
B) Prayer is valid
C) Prayer is valid only if said silently
D) Prayer is invalid unless the sneezer is also praying

Key Takeaway
Responding to a sneeze with 'yarhamuka Allah' invalidates prayer because it is customary speech; saying 'alhamdulillah' does not invalidate as it is dhikr.

Giving a Cue (Talqin) in Prayer: The Condition of Repetition
Ruling with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on talqin to a non-imam, distinguish between Imam Muhammad's two transmissions, and apply the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A single cue in prayer is pardoned; repetition invalidates, according to the relied-upon view.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of giving a verbal cue (talqin) to another praying person. While the general rule classifies such speech as kalam al-nas (human speech) which invalidates prayer, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani has two conflicting transmissions. Understanding how the school resolves this ikhtilaf is essential for accurate fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon and its default hukm. Here, the act is giving a cue (talqin) to a non-imam while in prayer. The default ruling is that it invalidates the prayer because it constitutes teaching and learning, which is considered kalam al-nas.
The ruling applies only when both the giver and receiver are in prayer; if one is not, it is outside this discussion.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi transmission
Identify the two transmissions from Imam Muhammad. In al-Mabsut, he stipulates repetition as a condition for invalidation. In al-Jami' al-Saghir, he does not, holding that any speech invalidates even if it is little.
Both are from Imam Muhammad, so this is an ikhtilaf within the same school.
3
Understand the reasoning behind each
For al-Mabsut: the act is not from the actions of prayer, so a small amount (once) is pardoned (qalil yu'fa). For al-Jami' al-Saghir: speech inherently disconnects prayer regardless of quantity, so no pardon.
The key difference is whether a small amount of extraneous speech is excused.
4
Identify the tarjih and mufta bihi
The mashayikh and the author of al-Hidayah prefer the view of al-Mabsut: a single cue is valid; repetition invalidates. This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
Tarjih is based on the principle that minor actions not contradicting the essence of prayer are pardoned.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, relying on the mufta bihi, a single cue does not break prayer, but multiple cues do. However, some judges follow the stricter view that any cue invalidates.
The practical consequence affects the validity of the prayer for both the giver and receiver.

Source Text

فاتح (اقمہ دینے والا ) نماز میں ہو اور مستفتح (تم منسوخ ہوتا والا ) نماز میں نہ ہو۔ پہلی صورت یعنی جب دونوں نماز میں نہ ہوں تو ہماری بحث سے خارج ہے اور دوسری قسم یعنی جب دونوں نہ اور جت ہوں تو اس کی دو صورتیں ہیں یا تو دونوں کی نماز متحدہ ہوگی بایں طور کہ مستفت یعنی اتمہ لینے والا امام ہو اور فاتن یعنی لقمہ دینے والا التقدير ) حضر ہو۔ یا دونوں کی نماز متحد نہ ہوگی پہلی صورت کو اگلی سطروں میں ذکر کریں گے۔ اور دوسری صورت میں یعنی جب دونوں کی نماز تھی ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کی نماز فاسد ہو جائے گی مستفتح کی بھی اور فاتح کی بھی کیونکہ یہ تعلیم اور تعلم ہے یعنی فاتح نے تعلیمی کوئی مملل متفتح نے مبسوط میں لکھا ہے کہ اگر لقمہ دے میں تکرار پایا گیا تو اس کی نماز فاسد ہوگی اور اگر تہران کیونکہ متقد 5 فاسد نہ ہوگی۔ اور دلیل یہ ذکر کی کہ لقمہ دینا ایک عمل ہے جو اعمال صلوۃ میں سے نہیں ہے اور منافی صلوۃ عمل اگر کثیر ہوتو مفسد نماز چھوڑ کر ہو گا اور اگر قلیل ہو تو مفسد نماز نہیں ہوتا پس ایک بار لقمہ دینا عمل قلیل ہے اور اس سے زائد عمل کثیر ہے اس وجہ سے امام محمد نے کہا کہ تر میں اگر تکرار پایا گیا تو نماز فاسد ہوگی ورنہ نہیں۔
Ruling on a praying person giving a cue to someone other than his imam. [Arabic:] And if he opens (i.e., gives a cue) ... meaning: the praying person gives a cue to someone other than his imam, because it is teaching and learning, so it becomes kalam al-nas. Then in al-Mabsut, repetition is stipulated because it is not from the actions of prayer, so a little is pardoned. But al-Jami' al-Saghir did not stipulate this because speech is inherently a disconnector, even if little. [Urdu explanation:]... If someone asks for a cue and the praying person gives it, his prayer becomes invalid. This means giving a cue to someone other than the imam. Because it is teaching and learning, it is counted as kalam al-nas. Imam Muhammad in al-Mabsut stipulated the condition of repetition, because this act is not from the actions of prayer, so a small amount is pardoned. But in al-Jami' al-Saghir, he did not stipulate this, because speech itself invalidates even if little. Explanation: Asking for a cue (istiftah) means requesting help... Then there are cases: either both are not in prayer (outside discussion), or both are in prayer. If both are in prayer, either their prayers are united (same congregation) or not. If united and the one asking is the imam and the one giving is a follower, this case will be mentioned later (valid). If not united, then both prayers become invalid because it is teaching and learning, thus kalam al-nas. The mashayikh and the author of al-Hidayah said that Imam Muhammad wrote in al-Mabsut: if the cue involves repetition, the prayer becomes invalid; if not, it does not because it is a small action, and a small action contrary to prayer is pardoned. So one cue is a small action; more than one is excessive.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a praying person giving a verbal cue (talqin) to someone else who is also praying but is not his imam. The general rule: such a cue invalidates the prayer because it constitutes teaching and learning, which is a form of kalam al-nas. However, Imam Muhammad introduces a nuance in al-Mabsut: if the cue is done only once (without repetition), it is considered a small action (qalil) and is pardoned because it is not from the actions of prayer; but if repeated, it becomes excessive (kathir) and invalidates. In al-Jami' al-Saghir, Imam Muhammad did not stipulate this condition and held that any speech, even once, invalidates. The passage then explains the various scenarios: both the giver and the receiver must be in prayer; if they are in the same congregation (mutahhidah), the follower may cue the imam validly (discussed later). If they are in separate prayers, both are invalidated as it is kalam al-nas. The relied-upon position is that of al-Mabsut: a single cue is pardoned; repetition invalidates.

Key Terms
استفتاح (istiftah): Asking for a cue or help regarding recitation.
فتح عليه (fatha 'alayhi): To give a cue by reciting the correct part to someone who is stuck.
تعلیم و تعلم (ta'lim wa ta'allum): Teaching and learning, which is considered a form of kalam al-nas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad in al-Mabsut, what condition makes giving a cue to another praying person invalidate the prayer?
A) The cue must be correct
B) The cue is repeated
C) The person giving the cue is the imam
D) The cue is given loudly

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is that a single verbal cue during prayer is pardoned; repetition invalidates.

Ruling on a Person Passing in Front of a Worshipper
States the ruling and inter-madhhab dispute with evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a woman, dog, or donkey passing in front of a worshipper, explain the conditions for sin, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the refutation of Aisha (رضي الله عنها).

Core Idea
A woman, dog, or donkey passing does NOT invalidate prayer, but the passer sins if within the place of prostration.

This passage addresses a common concern for worshippers: does someone passing in front of you break your prayer? The Hanafi school provides a clear and nuanced answer, distinguishing between the validity of the prayer and the sin of the passer. We will also examine the inter-madhhab dispute with the Shafi'i and Hanbali schools, focusing on the key hadith evidence and the powerful refutation by Aisha (رضي الله عنها).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core ruling: passing does not invalidate prayer, but the passer is sinful when conditions are met.
The prayer remains sahih (valid); the ruling is based on general hadith and the practice of the Prophet ﷺ.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Shafi'i school and Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal hold that the passing of a woman, dog, or donkey invalidates prayer (batil).
They rely on the hadith of Abu Dharr (رضي الله عنه).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the hadith 'Taqta' al-mar'atu al-salah wa al-kalbu wa al-himar' (a woman, dog, and donkey cut off prayer).
This is the primary evidence for the opposing view.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Aisha (رضي الله عنها) refuted this interpretation, stating that the Prophet ﷺ prayed while she lay in front of him. If lying down (greater obstruction) does not invalidate, then passing (lesser) a fortiori does not.
This uses qiyas (analogy) with a stronger case to refute the opposing hadith.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: In the Hanafi school, the worshipper continues praying; in the Shafi'i school, the prayer is invalid and must be repeated.
This affects whether one should interrupt prayer or not.

A woman passing in front of the worshipper does not invalidate prayer because of his (ﷺ) saying: 'Nothing passing cuts off the prayer.' But the passer is sinful because of his (ﷺ) saying: 'If the one passing in front of the worshipper knew what sin is upon him, he would stop for forty.' And he is sinful only when he passes in his place of prostration, as it is said, and there is no barrier between them, and the limbs of the passer are opposite his limbs, if he is praying on a platform. ... The issue: A woman passing in front of the worshipper does not invalidate prayer, whether she is menstruating or not; similarly, the passing of a donkey and a dog does not invalidate prayer. The Shafi'is say that the passing of these three invalidates prayer. The famous narration from Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal is the same. The Shafi'is' evidence is the Prophet's saying: 'The woman, the dog, and the donkey cut off the prayer.' This hadith is from Abu Dharr (رضي الله عنه). ... The response from Aisha (رضي الله عنها) ... The answer to the objection: If lying down does not invalidate prayer, then passing (which is less) is even less likely to invalidate. The majority's evidence is the Prophet's saying: 'Nothing passing cuts off the prayer; repel it as much as you can, for it is the devil.' But certainly, the passer in front of the worshipper is sinful. Because the Prophet (ﷺ) said: 'If the one passing in front of the worshipper knew what sin is upon him, he would stop for forty.' The narrator said: I do not know if forty years, forty months, or forty days. Some said that the hadith of Abu Hurairah (رضي الله عنه) authentically proves that forty years is meant.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. It begins with the Hanafi ruling: a woman (or dog or donkey) passing in front of a worshipper does not invalidate the prayer, based on the general hadith 'la yaqta' al-salah marur shay''. However, the passer is sinful if they pass within the place of prostration without a barrier. The sin is severe, as indicated by the hadith of 'forty' (interpreted as forty years). The condition for sin is that the passer crosses directly in front of the worshipper's place of prostration, with no barrier, and their limbs align. The Shafi'is and Ahmad ibn Hanbal hold that the passing of a woman, dog, or donkey invalidates prayer, citing the hadith of Abu Dharr. The Hanafi response, via Aisha (رضي الله عنها), is that she denied this interpretation, noting that the Prophet (ﷺ) prayed while she lay in front of him, and that did not invalidate his prayer. To the objection that Aisha's narration is about lying down, not passing, the Hanafis reply that if lying down (which involves more obstruction) does not invalidate, then passing (lesser obstruction) a fortiori does not. The majority's proof is the generic hadith that nothing cuts off prayer, but the passer is sinful. The exact duration of the sin (forty years/months/days) is not specified, but the authentic opinion is forty years.

Key Terms
مار (mārr): A person or animal passing in front of a praying person.
موضع سجود (mawḍiʿ al-sujūd): The place of prostration, i.e., the area from the worshipper's feet to the spot where the forehead rests in prostration.
آثم (āthim): Sinful; the passer bears sin even though the prayer is not invalidated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a woman passes in front of a man praying in the desert without a barrier, what is the ruling?
A) The prayer is invalid and must be repeated.
B) The prayer is valid but the woman is sinful if she passed within the place of prostration.
C) The prayer is valid and the woman is not sinful because it is a public area.
D) The prayer becomes makruh but not batil.

Key Takeaway
Passing in front of a worshipper does NOT invalidate prayer, but it is a major sin for the passer when done within the place of prostration without a barrier.

Facing the qibla with private parts in the toilet
Ruling with two narrations on facing/turning back to qibla during elimination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on facing the qibla while relieving oneself and distinguish between the two narrations on turning one's back.

Core Idea
Facing the qibla while relieving oneself is makruh; turning the back is subject to two narrations — makruh in one, not in another.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses the etiquette of relieving oneself with respect to the qibla. It clarifies that facing the qibla directly with the private parts is disliked due to prophetic prohibition. However, there is internal disagreement on whether turning one's back is also disliked. This lesson will help you understand the Hanafi position and the reasoning behind the two narrations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: facing the qibla with private parts while relieving oneself. The ruling is makruh (disliked).
The basis is a hadith prohibition.
2
Examine the proof
The dalil is a hadith forbidding facing the qibla. In Hanafi usul, such a prohibition indicates makruh, not haram, because the transmission is zanni.
The proof strengthens the ruling as it is explicit.
3
Differentiate the two narrations on istidbar
For turning one's back, one narration says it is makruh due to disrespect; another says it is not makruh because the private parts do not directly face the qibla.
The second narration focuses on the absence of direct facing.
4
Identify the mufta bihi
The preferred (mufta bihi) position: facing is makruh; turning the back is permissible, though caution is better.
Later Hanafi scholars prefer the view that istidbar is not makruh.
5
Note the practical consequence
In daily life, one should avoid facing the qibla when relieving oneself. If the toilet is oriented such that one's back is to the qibla, it is acceptable.
The ruling also applies to modern toilets that may be aligned with the qibla.

فصل
خارج نماز کے مکروہات کا بیان
بیت الخلاء میں فرج کے ساتھ استقبال قبلہ اور استد با رقبلہ مکروہ ہے
بكره استقبال القبلة بالفرج فى الخلاء لانه عليه السلام نهى عن ذلك والاستدبار يكره في رواية لما فيه من ترك التعظيم ولا يكره في رواية لان المستدبر فرجه غير موازى للقبلة وما ينحط منه ينحط الى الارض بخلاف المستقبل لان فرجة موازلها وما ينحط منـه يـنـحـط اليها رجمہ یہ فصل ہے۔ اور مکروہ ہے بیت الخلاء میں شرمگاہ کے ساتھ قبلہ کا رخ کرنا کیونکہ حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم نے اس سے منع فرمایا ہے اور ایک وایت میں استند بار بھی مکروہ ہے کیونکہ اس میں بھی ترک تعظیم ہے اور ایک روایت میں مکروہ نہیں ہے کیونکہ استند بار کرنے والا اس حال ما کہ اس کی شرمگاہ متوازی قبلہ نہیں ہے اور جو کچھ شرمگاہ سے گرتا ہے وہ زمین کی طرف گرتا ہے برخلاف استقبال قبلہ کرنے والے کے کینکہ اس کی شرمگاہ تو متوازی قبلہ ہے اور جو کچھ شرمگاہ سے گرتا ہے وہ قبلہ رخ جاتا ہے۔ تشریح قبل میں مکروہات نماز کا بیان تھا اس فصل میں خارج نماز کے مکروہات کا بیان ہے مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ قضاء حاجت یعنی پیشاب پائخانہ
Section: Statement of disliked actions outside prayer. It is disliked to face the qibla with the private parts in the toilet, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade that. And turning the back to the qibla is disliked in one narration because it involves lack of respect, and it is not disliked in another narration because the one turning his back has his private parts not facing the qibla, and what falls from them falls to the ground, unlike the one facing [the qibla] because his private parts are facing it and what falls from them falls towards the qibla. Explanation: Previously there was a statement of disliked actions in prayer; in this section there is a statement of disliked actions outside prayer. The issue is that relieving oneself, i.e., urination and defecation...
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the etiquette of relieving oneself with respect to the qibla. The author states that it is disliked (makruh) to face the qibla with one's private parts while in the toilet, as the Prophet forbade it. As for turning one's back to the qibla (istidbar), there are two narrations: one holds it is disliked because it is disrespectful; the other holds it is not disliked because the private parts are not directly facing the qibla, and the excretion falls to the ground, not towards the qibla. The preferred view in the Hanafi school is that facing the qibla is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) or at least makruh tanzihi, while turning the back is either permissible or mildly disliked depending on the narration. The chapter then continues to explain that this relates to actions outside prayer.

Key Terms
استقبال القبلة بالفرج (istiqbal al-qiblah bil-faraj): Facing the qibla with one's private parts while relieving oneself.
استدبار القبلة (istidbar al-qiblah): Turning one's back to the qibla while relieving oneself; two narrations on its ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on turning one's back to the qibla while relieving oneself?
A) It is forbidden (haram).
B) It is disliked (makruh) without exception.
C) There are two narrations: one says makruh, one says not makruh.
D) It is permissible (mubah) under all circumstances.

Key Takeaway
Facing the qibla while relieving oneself is makruh; turning the back is permissible in the preferred Hanafi view, but caution is better.

Taharah — Purification
beginner
6 min
Ruling on Facing the Qibla While Relieving Oneself
Explains the disliked ruling of facing or turning one's back to the Qibla during urination/defecation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on facing and turning one's back to the Qibla during relieving oneself, and distinguish between the two narrations on istidbar.

Core Idea
Facing the Qibla while relieving oneself is makruh tahrimi; turning your back is disputed and often not disliked.

In this lesson, we examine the ruling on directing one's private parts toward or away from the Qibla during urination or defecation. While the prohibition on facing the Qibla is clear from the hadith, the Hanafi school records two positions on turning one's back, requiring careful analysis of the underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is relieving oneself (urination/defecation) while facing the Qibla with one's private part (istiqbal) or turning one's back to it (istidbar). Determine the hukm level for each.
The text states that istiqbal is makruh; istidbar has two narrations.
2
Examine the primary proof
What is the textual evidence? The hadith from Salman (RA) narrated by Abu Dawud prohibits facing the Qibla with feces or urine. How does this prove the ruling? The explicit prohibition (nahi) indicates tahrim in Hanafi usul.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), so the ruling is makruh tahrimi, not haram.
3
Distinguish between istiqbal and istidbar
Why are they treated differently? The reasoning: when facing, the private part is parallel to the Qibla, and the discharge falls towards it. When turning the back, the private part is not aligned, and the discharge falls to the ground, so no disrespect occurs.
This distinction is central to the second narration on istidbar.
4
Analyze the intra-madhhab difference
What are the two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa? One says istidbar is also makruh due to abandonment of reverence; the other says it is not makruh. Understand the basis of each.
The text favors the second narration by providing reasoning.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Which position is relied upon for fatwa? The second narration (not makruh) is preferred because the private part and discharge are not directed towards the Qibla, so there is no parallel to the prohibition of facing.
This is the operative ruling in the Hanafi school today.

Chapter: The mention of disliked outside prayer. Facing the Qibla with the private part in the toilet and turning the back to the Qibla is disliked. It is disliked to face the Qibla with the private part in the toilet because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade that. Turning the back is disliked in one narration because there is abandonment of reverence, and in another narration it is not disliked because the one turning his back, his private part is not parallel to the Qibla, and what falls from it falls to the ground, unlike the one facing, because his private part is parallel to it and what falls from it falls towards it. This is a chapter. And it is disliked to face the Qibla with the private part in the toilet because the Prophet forbade it. In one narration, turning the back is also disliked because it involves abandonment of reverence, and in one narration it is not disliked because the one turning his back, his private part is not parallel to the Qibla, and what falls falls to the ground, unlike the one facing. Commentary: Previously there was mention of disliked in prayer; in this chapter, the mention of disliked outside prayer. The issue is that at the time of relieving oneself (urination/defecation), facing the Qibla with one's private part is makruh tahrimi, whether in an open field or in settlement, whether there is a wall in front or not; in any case, it is makruh tahrimi. The proof is that the Prophet forbade it. As narrated from Salman: someone said to him, 'Your Prophet has taught you everything, even how to relieve yourselves.' He said: 'Yes, he forbade us from facing the Qibla with feces or urine.' (Abu Dawud). Another narration of Abu Dawud: 'When you go to the toilet, do not face the Qibla with feces or urine, but face east or west.' Keep in mind that the command 'but face east or west' is specifically for the people of Madinah because the Kaaba is to the south of Madinah. For us Indians, this command is not applicable; rather, we should face north or south when relieving ourselves. Regarding turning the back to the Qibla, there are two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa: in one, turning the back also involves abandonment of reverence; in the other, it is not disliked because the private part does not face the Qibla and what falls goes to the ground. This matter has great detail, the field of which is the books of Hadith. Wait for the day you will hear this important issue in the year of Hadith study. Jamil Ahmad.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding facing or turning one's back to the Qibla while relieving oneself. It first states that facing the Qibla with the private part is makruh due to the Prophet's forbiddance. Regarding turning the back, there are two narrations: one considers it makruh due to lack of reverence; the other does not, as the private part is not aligned with the Qibla and the discharge falls to the ground, not towards the Qibla. The text then details the ruling as makruh tahrimi for facing, citing the hadith from Salman and the general command to face east or west. It clarifies that this command is specific to people of Madinah; for others, they should avoid facing the Qibla in any direction. A final note mentions two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa on turning the back, with the second view (not disliked) being more practical as the private part and discharge are not directed towards the Qibla.

Key Terms
استقبال القبلة (istiqbal al-qiblah): Facing the Qibla (Kaaba) with one's private parts while relieving oneself.
استدبار القبلة (istidbar al-qiblah): Turning one's back to the Qibla while relieving oneself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Facing Qibla While Relieving Oneself: Hanafi Ruling and Internal Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on facing Qibla during excretion with hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on facing the qibla while relieving oneself and distinguish between the two narrations of Imam Abu Hanifa regarding turning one's back to the qibla.

Core Idea
Facing the qibla during excretion is makruh tahrimi; turning your back is disputed even within the Hanafi school.

This lesson addresses a common daily-life issue: the etiquette of relieving oneself with respect to the qibla. The Hanafi school, relying on hadith, rules that facing the qibla with the private parts is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi). However, there is an internal difference of opinion on turning one's back to the qibla. Understanding this distinction is important for proper practice and avoiding unnecessary sin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the specific act being ruled upon and its hukm level. The act is facing the qibla with the private part during urination/defecation. The ruling is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked).
Makruh tahrimi is a Hanafi category between haram and makruh tanzihi.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited in the text. The author cites a hadith from Salman al-Farsi in Abu Dawud where the Prophet forbade facing the qibla during such acts.
The hadith is ahad, so its transmission is zanni.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith yields the ruling. The explicit prohibition (نهى) indicates the act is forbidden, but the Hanafi school downgrades it to makruh tahrimi due to other considerations (e.g., the hadith's zanniyyah).
The imperative of prohibition in Hanafi usul usually indicates tahrim, but here it is interpreted as karahah tahrimiyyah.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Observe the dispute within the school regarding istidbar (turning one's back). Imam Abu Hanifa has two narrations: one considers it makruh, the other does not.
This ikhtilaf is on a secondary issue; the base ruling on istiqbal is agreed upon.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence. In practice, a Hanafi may follow either view, but the text leans toward the second (not makruh) as stronger.
The second narration argues that the private parts do not face the qibla, so no disrespect.

سامنے ہوگی۔ اور جو کچھ پیشاب کرنے میں شرمگاہ سے گرتا ہے وہ قبلہ رخ ہو کر گرے گا۔ اس لئے استقبال قبلہ کومکروہ قرار دیا گیا۔ مسئلہ میں بہت تفصیل جس کا میدان سن کی کتابیں ہیں اس دن کا انتظار فر مائیے جب آپ دورہ حدیث کے سال اس اہم مسئلہ یکره ان يـ
سماعت فرمائیں گے۔ جمیل احمد
Chapter on what invalidates prayer and what is disliked in it. At the time of relieving oneself, facing the qibla with one's private part is makruh tahrimi, whether in an open field or in a settlement, even if there is no barrier in front; in any case it is makruh tahrimi. The proof is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited this. As the hadith: from Salman (that someone said) 'Your Prophet has taught you everything, even how to defecate.' He said: 'Yes, he forbade us from facing the qibla when defecating or urinating.' (Abu Dawud). Another narration: 'When you go to the toilet, do not face the qibla with your front or back, but face east or west.' This command to face east or west is specifically for the people of Madinah because the Kaaba is to the south; for us Indians, this command does not apply. For us, the ruling on turning one's back to the qibla (istidbar) has two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa. According to one, it is also a lack of respect. According to the other, it is not makruh, because when a person sits with his back to the qibla, his private part does not face the qibla, and whatever falls from it falls to the ground; the urine stream goes away from the qibla. Unlike facing the qibla, where the private part faces the qibla and what falls goes towards the qibla. Therefore, facing the qibla is declared makruh. This topic has much detail; the field of hadith books contains it; wait for the day when you will hear this important issue during the hadith course. Jamil Ahmad.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah discusses the ruling of facing the qibla while relieving oneself. It is a commentary on the Hidayah. The author first states the ruling: it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) to face the qibla with your private part when urinating or defecating, regardless of location. He then provides the proof: the hadith of Salman al-Farsi, where the Prophet forbade facing the qibla during such acts, and another hadith instructing to face east or west. The commentator clarifies that the command to face east or west was specific to the people of Madinah, as the Kaaba was to their south. For Indians (and by extension those in similar directions), the ruling does not apply literally. Instead, the commentator notes an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa has two narrations on turning one's back to the qibla. One considers it also makruh due to disrespect, while the other does not, reasoning that the private part does not face the qibla. The commentator favors the latter view based on the reasoning. This passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute with proof, as it presents two views within the Hanafi school and provides textual evidence.

Key Terms
مکروہ تحریمی (makruh tahrimi): A prohibition that is established by a zanni (non-definitive) evidence; it is sinful to commit but less severe than haram.
استقبال قبلہ (istiqbal al-qiblah): Facing the qibla with the front of the body, specifically the private parts, during urination/defecation.
استدبار قبلہ (istidbar al-qiblah): Turning one's back to the qibla during excretion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on turning one's back to the qibla when relieving oneself according to one of Imam Abu Hanifa's narrations?
A) It is haram.
B) It is makruh tahrimi.
C) It is not makruh.
D) It is mubah.

Key Takeaway
Facing the qibla during excretion is makruh tahrimi; turning one's back is subject to two narrations, with the preferred view being not makruh.

Ruling on the Roof of a Mosque
States that the roof of a mosque has the same ruling as the mosque itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the mosque's roof according to the Hanafi school and apply it to specific acts such as intercourse, urination, prayer, i'tikaf, and the state of janabah.

Core Idea
The roof of a mosque has the same ruling as the mosque itself.

This lesson addresses a frequent practical question: what acts are permitted or prohibited on the roof of a mosque? The text establishes a clear principle: the roof is legally part of the mosque. Therefore, acts that are prohibited inside the mosque—like sexual intercourse, urination, and defecation—are also makruh tahrimi on the roof. The ruling extends to other scenarios: praying behind an imam on the roof is valid, i'tikaf is not broken by climbing onto the roof, and a junub may not stand on the roof. Understanding this principle avoids common misconceptions that treat the roof as a separate, less sacred space.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling
Read the passage to determine the core ruling about acts on the mosque roof. Look for the terms 'makruh tahrimi' and the specific acts mentioned: intercourse, urination, defecation.
The passage uses the phrase 'مکروہ تحریمی ہے' to indicate the level of prohibition.
2
Extract the underlying principle
The text states: 'لان سطح المسجد له حكم المسجد' (because the roof of the mosque has the ruling of the mosque). This is the key legal principle—the roof shares the same legal status as the interior.
This principle is the basis for all subsequent rulings.
3
Apply the principle to the specific acts
From the principle, deduce the rulings for the listed acts. Confirm that the text explicitly includes intercourse, urination, and defecation as makruh tahrimi. Then note the implicit extension: any act prohibited inside the mosque is also prohibited on the roof.
Acts that are haram inside (like zina) would also be haram on the roof, but the text focuses on acts that are makruh tahrimi.
4
Identify the additional corollaries
The passage also addresses three distinct scenarios: praying behind an imam on the roof (validity of iqtida), effect on i'tikaf (not invalidated by climbing), and prohibition for a junub to stand on the roof. Read each and note how they flow from the same principle.
These corollaries show the scope of the principle.
5
Formulate a general rule
Synthesize your understanding into a concise rule: 'Anything that is prohibited or disliked inside the mosque carries the same ruling when done on its roof, unless there is specific evidence to the contrary.'
This aligns with the Hanafi position that the roof is an integral part of the mosque.

On the roof of the mosque, intercourse, urination, defecation are makruh tahrimi. ... because the roof of the mosque has the ruling of the mosque. ... Trumping of someone who is below the roof is valid. I'tikaf is not invalidated by climbing onto the roof. It is not permissible for a junub to stand on it. Intercourse, urination, and defecation on the roof are disliked because the roof has the ruling of the mosque. ...

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of the mosque's roof. The text states that the roof shares the same legal status as the mosque itself. Therefore, acts that are prohibited inside the mosque—such as sexual intercourse, urination, and defecation—are also makruh tahrimi on the roof. The commentator provides additional points: praying behind an imam on the roof is valid, i'tikaf is not nullified by going onto the roof, and a junub may not stand on it. The reasoning is based on the principle that the roof is an integral part of the mosque. This is a ruling with implicit proof by analogy to the mosque's interior.

Key Terms
سطح المسجد (sath al-masjid): The roof of the mosque, which has the same legal ruling as the mosque itself.

Key Takeaway
The mosque roof shares the same legal status as the interior, so all acts prohibited inside are also prohibited on the roof, and acts like i'tikaf are not broken by climbing onto it.

Ruling on Urinating on Roof of House with Prayer Room
Distinguishes a home prayer room from a public mosque regarding impurity on roof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on impurity on the roof of a house with a home prayer room and distinguish between a home prayer room and a public mosque in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A home prayer room is not a real mosque; impurity on its roof is permissible.

A common question arises: if a designated prayer area exists in a house, does the entire house become sanctified like a mosque? This lesson examines a clear Hanafi ruling that distinguishes between the legal status of a public masjid and a private prayer space. You will learn why impurity on the roof is allowed and the evidence for this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Read the passage and determine what act is being ruled upon and what the hukm level is (permissible, impermissible, etc.).
The act is urinating on the roof of a house with a prayer room; the ruling is permissible (la ba's).
2
Identify the Key Distinction
Note the reason given: the prayer room does not take the ruling of a mosque. Understand the legal difference between a home prayer room (masjid al-bayt) and a public mosque.
A home prayer room can be sold and inherited, unlike a real mosque.
3
Examine the Encouragement
The passage mentions that making a prayer room in the home is mustahabb, citing Quran and hadith. Separate the recommendation from the legal ruling.
Encouragement does not change the legal status.
4
Apply the Ruling
Consider a scenario: someone's house has a corner designated for salah. Can they clean a toilet on the same roof? The Hanafi ruling says yes.
The ruling is based on the absence of full mosque sanctity.

And there is no harm in urinating on the roof of a house in which there is a mosque (prayer room). And what is meant is a place designated for prayer in the house, because it does not take the ruling of a mosque, even though we are encouraged to make mosques in our homes. ... The issue is that if a place for prayer is designated in a house, there is no objection to urinating/defecating on its roof. This is because that place does not have the ruling of a true mosque; it can be sold and inherited. But we are encouraged to make mosques in our homes. It is mustahabb for every person to designate a place in his home for prayer, so that he can pray sunan and nawafil there. Allah said in the story of Musa: 'And make your houses a qibla.' And the Prophet said: 'Do not make your houses graves.' The meaning is: do not abandon prayer in your homes, making them like graveyards; rather, pray in them and worship Allah.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage clarifies that a designated prayer area within a private home does not have the same legal sanctity as a public mosque. Therefore, it is permissible to urinate or defecate on the roof of that house, even if the prayer area is directly below. The text explains that such a place is not considered a true mosque; it can be sold and inherited, and acts of worship performed there are merely recommended. The commentator cites Quranic and Prophetic encouragements to establish prayer in homes but distinguishes between a home prayer room and a masjid proper. This is a ruling_statement with supporting evidence from general principles.

Key Terms
مسجد البيت (masjid al-bayt): A designated prayer area in a house, which does not have the full legal rulings of a public mosque.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A designated prayer area in a private home does not acquire the sanctity of a public mosque, so actions like urinating on the roof or selling the house remain permissible.

Prayer Area in Home Does Not Have Ruling of Mosque
Clarifies that a designated prayer room does not take the ruling of a mosque.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on urinating on the roof of a house with a prayer area and explain the Hanafi legal basis for distinguishing between a designated musalla and a public mosque.

Core Idea
A designated prayer area in a home does not carry the same rulings as a public mosque—it remains private property.

Have you ever wondered whether a prayer room in your home has the same sanctity as a mosque? This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a practical question: is it permissible to urinate on the roof of a house that contains a designated prayer area? The answer reveals a key Hanafi principle—a home musalla does not take the full legal ruling of a masjid. We will explore the evidence from Quran and Sunnah and understand why this distinction matters for daily life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
First, identify what act is being discussed and what the ruling (hukm) is. The act is urinating on the roof of a house that contains a prayer area. The ruling is stated as 'no harm' (la ba's), meaning it is permissible (mubah), not even disliked.
2
Understand the Key Distinction
The permissibility hinges on the distinction between a full-fledged mosque (masjid) and a designated prayer area in a home (musalla). The text explains that the home prayer area 'has not taken the ruling of the mosque'—it remains private property, not public endowment.
This distinction has practical consequences: such a musalla can be sold and inherited, unlike a waqf mosque.
3
Locate the Proofs
The text provides two proofs: (1) Quran 10:87, where Allah tells Musa and Harun to 'make your houses a qibla' (place of prayer), implying that homes become places of worship without becoming full mosques. (2) Hadith: 'Do not make your houses graves,' encouraging prayer at home. Both proofs show that home prayer is encouraged but does not elevate the space to the legal status of a masjid.
The Quranic verse is of qati' thubut (certain transmission) but the dalala (indication) is zanni—it suggests but does not explicitly state the ruling.
4
Apply the Ruling and Note Practical Implications
Since the prayer area is not a mosque, acts that would be prohibited in a mosque (like urination) are permissible on the roof above it. However, the text also notes it is mustahabb to designate a prayer area at home. The ruling covers even defecation on the roof, as the text states.
A real mosque (public waqf) would prohibit such acts entirely.

ولابأس بالبول فوق بيت فيه مسجد و المراد ما اعد للصلوة فى البيت لانه لم يأخذ حكم المسجد وان لدينا اليه.
زیمہ اور ایسے گھر کی چھت پر پیشاب کرنے میں کوئی مضائقہ نہیں ہے جس گھر میں مسجد ہوا اور مرادوہ جگہ ہے جو گھر میں نماز کے لئے ر کر لی گئی ہو کیونکہ اس نے مسجد کا حکم نہیں لیا اگر چہ ہم کو گھروں میں مسجد بنانے کی ترغیب دی گئی ہے۔
کرنا مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر گھر میں نماز کی کوئی جگہ مقرر کر لی جائے تو اس گھر کی چھت پر پیشاب پاخانہ کرنے میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے۔ ہنا یہ ہے کہ اس جگہ کو حقیقی مسجد کا حکم نہیں دیا جائے گاحتیٰ کہ اس کو بیچا بھی جاسکتا ہے اور اس میں وراثت بھی جاری ہو گی لیکن ہم کو گھروں می مسجد بنانے کی ترغیب دی گئی ہے چنانچہ ہر انسان کے لئے مستحب ہے کہ وہ اپنے گھر میں نماز کے لئے کوئی جگہ مقرر کر لے تا کہ اس میں مسن اور نوافل ادا کرے، اللہ تعالیٰ نے حضرت موسٹی کے قصہ میں فرمایا ہے۔ وَاجْعَلُوا بُيُوتَكُمْ قِبلة" اور اپنے گھروں کو قبلہ بناؤ ، قبلہ تین اپنے گھروں میں نماز کی جگہ مقرر کر لو اور حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا لا تتخذوا بيوتكم قبرا اپنے گھروں کو قبرستان نہ بناؤ۔ مراد یہ ہے کہ گھروں میں نماز ترک کر کے ان کو قبرستان جیسی جگہ نہ بناؤ ، بلکہ گھروں میں نماز پڑھو۔ اور اللہ کی عبادت کرو۔
And there is no harm in urinating on a house that contains a mosque, and what is meant is that which is prepared for prayer in the house, because it has not taken the ruling of the mosque, even though we have been encouraged to do so. And there is no difficulty in urinating on the roof of such a house that has a mosque, and what is meant is the place that has been designated for prayer in the house, because it has not taken the ruling of the mosque, even though we have been encouraged to make mosques in houses. The issue is that if a place for prayer is designated in the house, there is no harm in urinating or defecating on its roof. The point is that this place will not be given the ruling of a real mosque, to the extent that it can be sold and inheritance applies to it, but we have been encouraged to make mosques in houses. Thus, it is mustahabb for every person to designate a place for prayer in his house so that he may perform sunan and nawafil prayers therein. Allah Almighty said in the story of Musa: 'And make your houses a qibla' meaning make your houses a place of prayer. And the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Do not make your houses graves' meaning do not abandon prayer in your houses making them like graveyards, but pray in your houses and worship Allah.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the permissibility of urinating on the roof of a house that contains a designated prayer area. The key distinction is that such a designated area (musalla) does not acquire the full legal ruling of a public mosque; it remains private property, so ordinary acts of impurity are not prohibited on its roof. The text provides proofs from the Quran (the story of Musa commanding to make homes places of prayer) and hadith (prohibition of making homes like graves), which encourage prayer at home without elevating the area to the status of a mosque. Thus, the act is permissible (mubah) and not even makruh.

Key Terms
مسجد (masjid): A designated place of worship, but in this context, a prayer area within a home that does not have the full legal status of a public mosque.
مکروہ (makruh): Disliked; in this context, the act of urinating on the roof is not even makruh, so it is permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, is it permissible to urinate on the roof of a house that has a designated prayer area inside?
A) Yes, it is permissible.
B) No, it is makruh.
C) No, it is haram.
D) Only permissible outside prayer times.

Key Takeaway
A designated prayer area in a home does not acquire the legal ruling of a mosque, so acts like urinating on the roof are permissible (mubah) even though the Quran and Sunnah encourage making homes places of prayer.

Ruling on Decorating Mosques with Plaster and Gold
Ruling on permissibility of mosque decoration with conditions on funding source.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mosque decoration, distinguish between permissible and impermissible funding sources, and evaluate the ikhtilaf with other schools.

Core Idea
Decorating mosques is permissible but not rewarded, provided it is funded from personal wealth, not waqf funds.

Many Muslims wish to beautify their mosques, but is this a commendable act of worship or a waste? The Hanafi school, as recorded in Al-Hidayah, provides a nuanced answer. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the conditions attached to it, and the scholarly disagreement surrounding mosque decoration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hukm from the matn
Read the author's statement: 'ولا بأس بان ينقش المسجد بالجص والساج وماء الذهب'. Identify the key phrase 'لا بأس' and note its legal weight in Hanafi fiqh.
The matn directly states the ruling, but understanding 'لا بأس' requires commentary.
2
Understand the nuance of 'la ba's'
The commentator explains that 'لا بأس' indicates no reward and no sin. Contrast this with acts that are mandub (recommended) or makruh (disliked).
This neutral ruling (mubah) is distinct from saying it is a qurba (devotion).
3
Distinguish between personal and waqf funding
The matn sets a critical condition: 'اذا فعل من مال نفسه' (if done from one's own wealth). If a mutawalli uses waqf funds, he becomes damin (liable). Identify the difference in ruling based on funding source.
The mutawalli's duty is only to maintain structural integrity, not beautify.
4
Compare Hanafi and opposing views
The commentary presents a dispute: some scholars consider decoration makruh based on hadith and early practice. Summarize the Hanafi proofs (Umar's action, encouragement of i'tikaf) and the opposing arguments (Ali's statement, Waleed's spending).
The Hanafi tarjih relies on the absence of a clear prohibition and the permissibility of beautification.
5
Apply the ruling to a modern scenario
Consider a case: A mosque committee uses donated funds to install gold-plated chandeliers. Determine the ruling and identify who would be liable if the donor intended the funds for maintenance or general mosque needs.
The key is tracing the source of funds and the intention of the donor or waqf founder.
