A Muslim guardian of a disbeliever will wash him, shroud him, and bury him. And if a disbeliever dies and he has a Muslim wali (guardian), then he washes him, shrouds him, and buries him. This is what was commanded to Ali regarding his father Abu Talib. But he washes it like washing a najis cloth, and wraps it in a rag, and a pit is dug without observing the sunnah of shrouding and lahd (niche), and it is not placed (in a proper manner), but rather thrown. Translation: When a disbeliever dies and he has a Muslim heir, then the Muslim gives him a bath, gives him a shroud, and buries him. Hazrat Ali was given a similar command regarding his father Abu Talib. But the bath is given the way a najis cloth is washed, and he is wrapped in one cloth, and a pit is dug without observing the sunnah of shrouding and lahd, and he is not placed but rather thrown. Explanation: The issue is that if a disbeliever dies and none of his disbelieving guardians are present, but there is a Muslim wali, i.e., a close relative of that disbeliever who is Muslim, then this Muslim washes him like a najis cloth, wraps him in one cloth, and puts him in a pit. The proof is that when Abu Talib died, Hazrat Ali informed the Prophet (ﷺ), and he said: 'Wash him, shroud him, and bury him, and do not initiate any matter until you come to me.' Meaning, do not pray over him. The intention of the Prophet (ﷺ) is not to perform the burial and shroud in the sunnah manner. This is what the author of Hidaya said: that the disbeliever's body is washed like a najis cloth, and simply wrapped in a cloth, and a pit is dug and he is thrown into it. And if the disbeliever's disbelieving guardians are present, then the Muslim should step aside and leave them to deal with him as they wish. In the text 'له ولي مسلم' the word wali refers to a close relative, because true guardianship (wilayah) does not exist between a Muslim and a disbeliever. Allah says: 'Do not take the Jews and Christians as guardians (awliya).'
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage deals with the funeral of a non-Muslim (kafir) when only a Muslim relative is available to handle the body. The ruling is that the Muslim may perform the essential acts: washing, enshrouding, and burying, but without any of the Islamic sunnah practices. The washing is minimal, like washing a najis cloth (i.e., just to clean, not ritual ghusl). The shroud is a single cloth (not the typical three-piece kafan). The grave is a simple pit without the niche (lahd). The body is simply thrown in, not placed with care. No janaza prayer is offered. The proof is from the incident of Abu Talib's death; the Prophet commanded Ali to wash, shroud, and bury him, but explicitly told him not to do anything else until meeting the Prophet, interpreted as refraining from prayer and sunnah rites. If the deceased has non-Muslim guardians alive, the Muslim should not interfere and let them handle the funeral according to their own customs. The term 'wali' in the text is clarified to mean a close relative by lineage, not a legal guardianship, because Muslims cannot be guardians of non-Muslims according to the Quranic prohibition.

Key Terms
ولي (wali): A close relative (by blood) who has authority to handle the funeral, but here it is limited to physical acts, not religious guardianship.
غسل الثوب النجس (ghusl al-thawb al-najis): Washing like a najis cloth; a simple washing to remove physical impurity, not a ritual ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How should a Muslim wash the body of a non-Muslim relative according to this passage?
A) With the same ghusl as a Muslim
B) Like washing a najis cloth
C) Not at all; it is forbidden
D) With a simple water pour without wiping

Key Takeaway
When a Muslim is the only available relative, he may give a non-Muslim relative a minimal burial (washed like a cloth, wrapped in one cloth, thrown into a pit) without any Islamic rites.

Definition, Conditions, and the Dispute on Prayer for the Martyr
Definition of martyr and dispute on prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of shahid, identify the conditions for not washing the martyr, and explain the Hanafi position on funeral prayer over the martyr in response to the Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
The martyr is not washed but is shrouded and prayed over, as a concession honoring his sacrifice.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah defines who is legally considered a shahid (martyr) and the associated funeral rulings. It clarifies that martyrs are not given ghusl, but are shrouded with their wounds and funeral prayer is performed for them. The passage also presents a key inter-madhhab dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i argues that no prayer is needed because the sword erases sins, while the Hanafi school insists on prayer as an act of honor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of persons who qualify as shahid
Read the definition: the polytheists killed him, or he is found on the battlefield with an effect (wound), or Muslims killed him unjustly without blood-money. Each category triggers the same funeral rulings.
The third category (killed by Muslims unjustly) is unique to Hanafis and extends the shahid ruling beyond battlefield deaths.
2
List the conditions for being treated like the martyrs of Uhud
From the hadith and the passage, extract the conditions: killed by iron (sharp weapon), killed unjustly, the person was pure (not in janabah), adult (baligh), and no financial compensation (diyah) is due.
The phrase 'in their meaning' (في معناه) links these conditions to the original Uhud martyrs.
3
Understand the three rulings: no ghusl, shroud with wounds, prayer
The passage states: 'يكفن ويصلى عليه ولا يغسل' – he is shrouded, prayer is said over him, and he is not washed. This is based on the Prophet's command: 'Wrap them with their wounds and their blood, and do not wash them.'
The shrouding is done with the bloodstained clothes; they are not removed.
4
Analyze the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i on funeral prayer
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the sword erases all sins, so the martyr is purified and does not need the intercession (shafa'ah) sought in funeral prayer. The Hanafi response: prayer is not only for forgiveness but to manifest honor (ikram), and the martyr is most deserving; even the sinless (Prophet, children) receive prayer.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence): in Hanafi school, prayer is fard kifayah; in Shafi'i, no prayer is offered for martyrs of battle.
5
Apply the rulings to a new scenario
Consider a Muslim adult killed unjustly with a knife (iron) in a robbery, no diyah due, and he was in a state of wudu. He qualifies as a shahid. His body should not be washed, but should be shrouded without removing bloodstained clothes, and the congregation must pray over him.
If he had been in janabah, he would need ghusl first; the condition of purity excludes janabah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الشهيد

الشهيد من قتله المشركون او وجد فى المعركة وبه اثر اوقتله المسلمون ظلما ولم يجب يقتله دية فيكفن ويصلى عليه ولا يغسل لأنه في معنى شهداء احد و قال صلى الله عليه وسلم فيهم زملوهم بكلو مهم ودما نهم ولا تغسلواهم فكل من قتل بالحديد ظلما وهو طاهر بالغ ولم يجب به عوض مالى فهو في معناه فيلحق بهم والمراد بالأثر الجراحة لانها دلالة القتل وكذا خروج الدم من موضع غير معتاد كالعين ونحوه والشافعي يخالفنا في الصلوة ويقول السيف محاء للذنوب فاغنى عن الشفاعة ونحن نقول الصلوة على الميت لاظهار كرامته والشهيد اولى بها والطاهر عن الذنوب لا يستغنى عن الدعاء كالنبي والصبي
Chapter of the Martyr. The martyr is the one whom the polytheists killed, or [he] is found in the battlefield and on him is an effect, or the Muslims killed him unjustly and no blood-money became due for his killing. So he is shrouded and prayer is said over him, and he is not washed, because he is in the meaning of the martyrs of Uhud. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said regarding them: 'Wrap them with their wounds and their blood, and do not wash them.' So whoever is killed unjustly with iron, while he is pure and adult, and no financial compensation became due for it, he is in their meaning, so he is attached to them. And by 'effect' is meant the wound because it indicates killing, likewise the exit of blood from an unusual place like the eye and the like. And al-Shafi'i disagrees with us regarding the prayer. He says: 'The sword is an eraser of sins, so it suffices from intercession.' And we say: 'Prayer over the deceased is to manifest his honor, and the martyr is more deserving of it. And the one pure from sins is not free from need of supplication, like the Prophet and the child.'
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah is a commentary on the chapter of the martyr (shahid) from al-Hidayah. It begins by defining the shahid: a Muslim who is killed by polytheists (mushriks) in battle, or found dead on the battlefield with visible wounds (athar), or killed unjustly by Muslims without blood-money (diyah) being wajib. In all these cases, the ruling is that the body is shrouded (kafan), funeral prayer (salat al-janazah) is performed, but the body is not given ghusl (ritual wash). The proof is the hadith of the martyrs of Uhud where the Prophet ordered them to be wrapped with their wounds and blood and not washed. The condition for attaching to the Uhud martyrs is: killed by a sharp iron weapon (hadid) unjustly, the person is pure (i.e. neither in janabah nor hayd), adult (baligh), and no financial compensation is due for his killing. The 'effect' (athar) refers to a wound that indicates killing, including blood from unusual places like the eye. The passage then presents the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the funeral prayer. Imam Shafi'i argues that the sword erases sins, so the martyr does not need intercession; hence no prayer is offered. The Hanafi response: prayer over the deceased is to honor them, and the martyr is more deserving of honor. Even a sinless person like the Prophet or a child is prayed over, proving that prayer is not only for forgiveness but also for honor and supplication.

Key Terms
شهيد (shahid): A person killed unjustly by a sharp weapon (iron) while pure and adult, with no financial compensation due; includes those killed by polytheists in battle, found dead with wounds, or killed unjustly by Muslims without diyah.
أثر (athar): Effect on the body indicating killing, typically a wound (jarh) or blood from an unusual place like the eye.
الحديد (al-hadid): Iron; a sharp weapon made of iron (e.g. sword, knife), which is a condition for attaching to the rulings of Uhud martyrs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for a person to be treated as a shahid with the ruling of no ghusl?
A) Killed by an iron weapon
B) Killed unjustly
C) Killed by polytheists only
D) The person was pure (not in a state of ritual impurity)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a shahid is not washed but is shrouded with wounds, and funeral prayer is wajib (fard kifayah) over him, contrary to the Shafi'i position that no prayer is offered.

Washing a Martyr in Janabah and Related Cases
Intra-Hanafi dispute on washing a martyr in janabah and related cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between a preventer (mani') and a remover (rafi') to determine when a martyr in a state of janabah, a menstruating woman, or a child must be washed.

Core Idea
Martyrdom prevents new obligations but does not remove existing ones — so a janabah martyr must be washed.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether martyrdom cancels the obligation of ghusl for those in a state of janabah, menstruation, or postpartum bleeding. The key is whether shahadah is a preventer (mani') or a remover (rafi'). We will follow the reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and see how the incident of Hanzalah provides the decisive proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the ikhtilaf
State the practical difference: Abu Hanifa says wash the janabah martyr; Sahibayn say do not wash. This is the core dispute.
The ikhtilaf also extends to a menstruating/postpartum woman after purity and a child martyr.
2
Understand the conceptual distinction
Mani' vs rafi': shahadah prevents future obligations but does not remove past ones. If a person was already in janabah, that obligation persists.
This is the usul principle underlying Abu Hanifa's position.
3
Examine the dalil
Abu Hanifa relies on the hadith of Hanzalah (washed by angels). The Sahibayn rely on the general hadith 'Do not wash the martyrs.' Note the wajh al-dalalah.
The general hadith is zanni in dalala; the specific incident provides takhsis.
4
Evaluate the objection and its answer
Objection: If washing is obligatory, why did the Prophet not repeat Hanzalah's wash? Answer: The angels' washing fulfilled the obligation, just as angels washed Adam.
This rebuttal preserves the principle that the obligation is real, not nullified.
5
Extend to other cases
Apply the same logic: menstruating/postpartum woman after cessation but before ghusl is washed (Abu Hanifa); child martyr is washed because no sin exists for sword to purify.
The Sahibayn say child is more deserving of the honor of not being washed.

Source Text

امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک اس کو نسل دیا جائے گا اور صاحبین نے کہا کہ اس کو نسل نہیں دیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ جو نسل جنابت کی وجہ سے واجب ہوا وہ موت سے ساقط ہو گیا۔ اور دوسرا غسل شہادت کی وجہ سے واجب نہیں ہے۔ تو بچہ شہداء احد کے معنی میں نہ ہوا۔ تشریح مسئله، جنبی مسلمان اگر شہید ہو گیا تو امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک اس کو نسل دیا جائے یہی امام احمد کا قول ہے اور صاحبین کے نزد یک غسل نہ دیا جائے۔ اس کے قائل امام نزدیک شہادت مانع وجوب غسل ہے رافع مغسل نہیں ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک غسل نہ دیا جائے کیونکہ اول تو موت کی وجہ سے ساقط ہو گیا اور ثانی شہادت کی وجہ سے واجب نہیں ہوا۔ اور ایک روایت کے مطابق اگر خون بند ہونے سے پہلے شہید ہوگئی تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک اس کو نسل نہیں دیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ خون منقطع ہونے سے پہلے اس پر غسل واجب ہی نہیں ہوا اور دوسری روایت کے مطابق غسل دیا جائے گا۔ یہی صحیح روایت ہے۔ کیونکہ موت کی وجہ سے انقطاع دم حاصل ہو گیا اور دم سائل انقطاع کے وقت غسل کو واجب کرتا ہے اور بچہ اگر شہید کر دیا گیا تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک اس کو نسل دیا جائے گا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک غسل نہ دیا جائے۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ شہید سے غسل کا ساقط ہونا اس لئے ہے تا کہ اس کی مظلومیت کا اثر باقی رہے۔ پس شہید کو نسل نہ دینا اس کے اکرام کے پیش نظر ہے اور بچہ کی مظلومیت زیادہ ہے لہذا بچہ اس کرامت کا زیادہ مستحق ہے۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ شہداء احد کے حق میں میں تلوار نسل سے کافی ہو گئی۔ کیونکہ تلوار گناہوں سے پاک کر دیتی ہے۔ یعنی شہداء احد کو نسل اس لئے نہیں دیا گیا کہ تلوار نے ان کو گناہوں سے پاک کر دیا ہے اور چونکہ بچہ پر کوئی گناہ نہیں ہے اس لئے بچہ شہداء احد کے معنی میں نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب شہدا ءواحد کے معنی میں نہ ہوا تو شہداء احد کی طرح بچہ سے غسل بھی ساقط نہ ہوگا بلکہ بچہ کونسل دیا جائے گا۔
Chapter of the Martyr. Necessity for martyrdom and Abu Hanifa: that martyrdom is known as a preventive not a remover, so it does not remove janabah. It is authentically reported that Hanzalah when he was martyred in a state of janabah, the angels washed him. And upon this same difference is the menstruating and postpartum woman when they become pure, and similarly before the cessation in the correct narration. And upon this same difference is the boy: according to them (the Sahibayn) the boy is more deserving of this honor; and according to him (Abu Hanifa) the sword sufficed for ghusl in the case of the martyrs of Uhud in the description of being a purification, and there is no sin upon the boy, so he is not included in their meaning. Translation: And if he is martyred in a state of janabah, According to Imam Abu Hanifa, he is to be washed, and the Sahibayn said he is not to be washed, because the washing that was obligatory due to janabah is nullified by death, and a second washing is not obligatory due to martyrdom. And Abu Hanifa's proof: that martyrdom is recognized as preventing the obligation of washing the deceased, not removing the obligation, so it does not remove janabah. And this is proven by the fact that when Hanzalah was martyred in janabah, the angels washed him. And upon this same difference is the menstruating and postpartum woman; when they become pure and are martyred before washing, and similarly before cessation according to the sound narration. And upon this same difference is the boy: the Sahibayn argue that the boy is more deserving of this honor; Abu Hanifa argues that the sword sufficed for washing in the case of the martyrs of Uhud, as the sword purifies from sins, and since the boy has no sin, he is not included in their meaning. Explanation of the issue: If a Muslim dies in janabah, according to Imam Abu Hanifa he is to be washed; this is also the saying of Imam Ahmad. According to the Sahibayn, he is not to be washed; this is the saying of Imam Shafi'i. The Sahibayn's proof: The washing that was obligatory due to janabah is nullified by death, because due to death he is no longer obligated for janabah ghusl, and a second washing, i.e., washing the deceased, is not obligatory due to martyrdom because martyrdom prevents the obligation of washing, because the Prophet ﷺ said regarding martyrs: 'Wrap them in their wounds, and do not wash them.' The hadith does not specify whether the martyr is in janabah or not. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof: Martyrdom prevents the obligation of washing the deceased, but if there was an existing obligation, it does not remove it. For example, if there is impurity on the martyr's clothes, it must be washed off, but the blood on his body need not be washed. Since martyrdom is not a remover, it does not remove janabah either. And when janabah is not removed, washing for janabah becomes obligatory upon the janabah martyr. This is supported by the incident of Hanzalah: when he was martyred, the angels washed him. The Prophet ﷺ asked his family about his state, his wife said he had intercourse with her, then when the call for battle came, he went without washing and was martyred. The Prophet said, 'This is the reason.' An objection is raised: Only humans are obligated to wash, not angels. So if washing the janabah martyr were obligatory, the Prophet would have ordered them to wash him again. The answer: The obligation is to wash him, regardless of who performs it. When the angels washed Adam, the obligation was fulfilled; the children of Adam did not repeat Adam's washing. If the angels' washing were insufficient, they would have repeated it, and the Prophet would have repeated Hanzalah's washing. This same difference applies to the menstruating and postpartum woman: if she becomes pure (blood stops) and is martyred before washing, according to Abu Hanifa she is washed because martyrdom prevents the obligation of washing, not removes it. According to the Sahibayn, she is not washed, because the obligation is nullified by death and not re-obligated by martyrdom. According to one narration, if she is martyred before the blood stops, Imam Abu Hanifa says she is not washed because before the blood stops, ghusl was not obligatory. According to another narration, she is washed; this is the correct narration, because death causes the blood to stop, and flowing blood obligates ghusl at the moment of cessation. And if a child is martyred, according to Imam Abu Hanifa he is washed; according to the Sahibayn, he is not washed. The Sahibayn's proof: Ghusl is omitted for the martyr so that the effect of his oppression remains; withholding washing is an honor, and a child's oppression is greater, so he is more deserving of this honor. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof: In the case of the martyrs of Uhud, the sword was sufficient for washing, because the sword purifies from sins. Since the child has no sin, he is not included in the meaning of the martyrs of Uhud. And when he is not included in their meaning, ghusl is not omitted from him like the martyrs of Uhud; rather, he is washed.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on washing a martyr who was in a state of janabah (major impurity). It is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn (Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad). The passage also extends the same difference to a menstruating woman, a postpartum woman, and a child. The author begins with the Arabic matn of al-Hidayah, then provides an Urdu commentary. The core issue is whether martyrdom (shahadah) prevents the obligation of ghusl or removes an already existing obligation. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that martyrdom is a mani' (preventer) of the obligation of ghusl, not a rafi' (remover). Therefore, if a person was already in a state of janabah and then became a martyr, the ghusl for janabah remains obligatory. He supports this with the hadith of Hanzalah, who was in janabah when martyred and was washed by angels. The Sahibayn argue that the ghusl for janabah is nullified by death because the person is no longer a legal subject (mukallaf), and martyrdom does not introduce a new obligation for ghusl, as the Prophet's command 'Do not wash the martyrs' is general. They also rely on the hadith 'Wrap them in their wounds.' The commentary then addresses a potential objection: if washing was obligatory, why did the Prophet not order Hanzalah to be washed again? The answer is that the obligation was fulfilled by the angels' washing, just as Adam was washed by angels. The same principle is applied to a menstruating or postpartum woman who becomes pure (blood stops) but has not yet performed ghusl before martyrdom. Abu Hanifa says she must be washed, while the Sahibayn say no. There is also a sub-issue regarding a woman martyred before the blood stops; the preferred view is that she is washed because death causes the blood to stop, triggering the obligation of ghusl. Finally, for a child martyr, Abu Hanifa argues that he must be washed because the child has no sin, unlike the martyrs of Uhud for whom the sword purifies sins. The Sahibayn argue that a child is more oppressed and thus more deserving of the honor of not being washed. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view, as it is the majority of the Hanafi school and supported by the incident of Hanzalah.

Key Terms
الشهادة (al-shahadah): Martyrdom in battle for the sake of Allah, which has specific rulings regarding washing and burial.
الجنابة (al-janabah): State of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl, typically due to sexual intercourse or emission.
الحائض والنفساء (al-ha'id wa al-nafsa'): The menstruating woman and the postpartum woman; after bleeding stops, they must perform ghusl before prayer, etc.
الصبي (al-sabi): A male child who has not reached puberty. In this context, a child martyr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the sahibayn, why should a martyr in janabah not be washed?
A) Because the hadith says so and death nullifies obligations.
B) Because janabah is not a sin.
C) Because the angels did not wash Hanzalah.
D) Because the sword purifies.

Key Takeaway
Martyrdom (shahadah) is a preventer (mani') of the obligation of ghusl, not a remover (rafi'), so a martyr in janabah remains obligated to ghusl and must be washed.

Removing Items Like Fur and Armor from the Martyr
Dispute over removal of extra garments from martyr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on which items are removed from a martyr, identify the opposing Shafi'i view, and evaluate the Hanafi reliance on qiyas in the case of conflicting hadith.

Core Idea
Items not of the shroud type must be removed from the martyr: fur coat, padded garment, weapon, and leather socks.

This passage from the Hidayah addresses a practical burial issue: what should be done with items like fur, padded clothing, weapons, and leather socks on the body of a martyr? The Hanafi school differentiates between the martyr's regular clothes (which remain) and these additional items, which must be removed because they are not part of the standard shroud. This ruling emerges from a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who held that nothing should be removed. The Hanafi proof relies on a specific hadith from Ibn Abbas and a qiyas (analogy) to the shroud when the primary texts appear conflicting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Determine what items are explicitly listed for removal and which items are left on the martyr.
The martyr's own clothes (thiyab) are not removed.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof
Study the hadith of Ibn Abbas about removing iron and leather from the martyrs of Uhud. Also understand the qiyas that these items are not of the type of shroud.
The hadith of Ibn Abbas is specific and commands removal of certain materials.
3
Understand the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i relies on the general command 'Zammiluhum' (wrap them in their clothes) which seems to prohibit removing any clothing.
This creates a conflict (ta'arud) between the two hadiths.
4
Analyze the Hanafi approach to conflict
The Hanafis consider the two hadiths to be in ta'arud (contradiction). They abandon both and resort to qiyas based on the effective cause (illa) that the items are not of the type of shroud.
This illustrates a key usul principle: when texts conflict, one may resort to qiyas.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis remove fur, padding, weapons, and leather socks; Shafi'is leave everything on the body.
This affects the actual burial procedure.

Source Text

The martyr's blood should not be wiped off, nor should his clothes be removed, excess items are removed. And his blood should not be washed and his clothes should not be removed because of the hadith we narrated. And removed from him are the fur garment, padding, weapon, and khuff (leather socks) because they are not of the type of the shroud. And they may increase or decrease whatever they wish to complete the shroud. Translation: The martyr's blood should not be washed, nor should his clothes be removed, because of the hadith we narrated. And removed from the martyr are the fur coat, padded items, weapon, and khuff, because these items are not of the type of shroud. And to complete the shroud, they may increase or decrease as they wish. Explanation: If the martyr is wearing leather clothing, fur, etc., or padded items, or a weapon and khuff, they should be removed. This is the position of the Hanafi scholars. Imam Shafi'i said that nothing should be removed from the martyr's body. Imam Shafi'i's proof is the saying of the Prophet ﷺ 'Zammiluhum (wrap them)' etc.: wrap the martyrs in their clothes, without specifying which clothes to remove. Therefore, the general command implies that no clothing should be removed. Our proof is the hadith of Ibn Abbas: He said that the Prophet ﷺ ordered regarding the slain of Uhud that iron (weapons) and leather be removed from them, and that they be buried with their blood and clothes. Apparently, these two hadiths conflict. So we abandon both and resort to qiyas. The qiyas is that such items are removed because they are not of the type of shroud.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules regarding what should be removed from the body of a martyr before burial. It is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is. The Hanafi position, derived from the Hidayah, is that the martyr's blood is not washed off, and his clothes are not removed. However, certain items that are not part of the standard shroud (kafan) must be removed: fur garments (farrow), padded garments (hashw), weapons (silah), and leather socks (khuff). These are removed because they are not of the same category as the shroud. The Hanafis rely on a hadith from Ibn Abbas that the Prophet ordered the removal of iron and leather from the martyrs of Uhud. Imam Shafi'i argues from the general hadith 'Wrap them in their clothes' which seems to prohibit removing anything. Since the two hadiths appear contradictory (ta'arud), the Hanafis set both aside and resort to qiyas (analogical reasoning). The effective cause (illa) is that these items are not part of the usual shroud, so they must be removed. The commentary also notes that additional cloth may be added or subtracted to complete the shroud as needed.

Key Terms
الفرو (al-farrow): A fur garment or leather coat worn for warmth.
الحشو (al-hashw): Padded garment filled with cotton or wool, like a quilted coat.
السلاح (al-silah): Weapon, such as sword, spear, etc.
الخف (al-khuff): Leather socks worn over the feet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It must be left on because all clothes remain on the martyr.
C) It is optional; the family can decide.
D) Only if the fur coat is torn.

Key Takeaway
According to the Hanafi school, items that are not part of the standard shroud (fur, padded garments, weapons, leather socks) must be removed from the martyr before burial.

Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Washing a Woman Martyr After Menses
Explains the difference between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn on washing a martyr who was in menstruation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on washing a woman martyr who was in a state of major impurity, and EXPLAIN the legal reasoning behind each position using the concepts of mani' and rafi'.

Core Idea
Martyrdom prevents (mani') but does not nullify (rafi') an existing obligation of ghusl — the key distinction between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

The general rule is that martyrs of the battlefield are not washed. However, an exception arises when the martyr was in a state of major impurity (janabah) before death. This lesson explores the intra-Hanafi dispute over a woman who becomes pure from menstruation or postnatal bleeding but is martyred before performing ghusl. The disagreement hinges on whether martyrdom nullifies an already established obligation or merely prevents a new one from arising.

Guided Path
1
State the general ruling on martyrs
Recall that a battlefield martyr (shahid) is not washed and is buried in his blood-stained clothes. This is the default rule.
The exception for janabah is a nuance.
2
Identify the exceptional case in dispute
A woman in menstruation/postnatal bleeding becomes pure (blood stops) but is martyred before performing ghusl. The question: should she be washed?
The dispute applies similarly to nifas and hayd.
3
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position and reasoning
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that she must be washed. His reasoning: martyrdom (shahadat) is a mani' (preventer) of the obligation of ghusl, not a rafi' (nullifier). Since the obligation was already established when the blood stopped, martyrdom does not remove it.
Two narrations exist regarding the case before blood stops: one says no wash, the other (sahih) says wash because death causes the blood to stop, making ghusl obligatory.
4
State the Sahibayn's position and reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that she is not washed. Their reasoning: death cancels all pending obligations, and martyrdom prevents any new obligation from arising. Thus, no ghusl is required.
This is based on the idea that martyrdom is a complete bar to new obligations.
5
Note the preferred narration and practical consequence
The author of Ashraf al-Hidayah declares the narration that requires washing as sahih (correct). Therefore, the mufta bihi position is that she is washed.
The thamarah al-khilaf: if the mufta bihi is followed, she receives ghusl; if the Sahibayn's view is taken, she is buried without washing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
یہی اختلاف حائضہ اور نفاس والی عورت میں ہے۔ یعنی اگر حیض یا نفاس کا خون منقطع ہو کر پاک ہو گئی اور ابھی غسل نہیں کیا اہی حالت میں شہید ہوگئی تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک غسل دیا جائے گا کیونکہ امام صاحب کے نزدیک شہادت مانع وجوب غسل ہے رافع مغسل نہیں ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک غسل نہ دیا جائے کیونکہ اول تو موت کی وجہ سے ساقط ہو گیا اور ثانی شہادت کی وجہ سے واجب نہیں ہوا۔ اور ایک روایت کے مطابق اگر خون بند ہونے سے پہلے شہید ہوگئی تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک اس کو نسل نہیں دیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ خون منقطع ہونے سے پہلے اس پر غسل واجب ہی نہیں ہوا اور دوسری روایت کے مطابق غسل دیا جائے گا۔ یہی صحیح روایت ہے۔ کیونکہ موت کی وجہ سے انقطاع دم حاصل ہو گیا اور دم سائل انقطاع کے وقت غسل کو واجب کرتا ہے
The same difference is regarding a menstruating woman and a woman in postnatal bleeding. That is, if the blood of menstruation or postnatal bleeding stops and she becomes pure, but she has not yet performed ghusl, and she is martyred in this state, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, she should be given a bath, because according to Imam Sahib, martyrdom prevents the obligation of ghusl, it does not nullify the obligation. And according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), she should not be given a bath, because first, due to death, the obligation fell away, and second, due to martyrdom, it did not become obligatory. And according to one narration, if she was martyred before the blood stopped, then according to Imam Sahib, she should not be given a bath, because before the blood stopped, ghusl was not obligatory upon her, and according to the other narration, she should be given a bath. This is the correct narration, because due to death, cessation of blood occurred, and flowing blood at the time of cessation makes ghusl obligatory.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the washing of a martyr who was previously in a state of major impurity (janabah) due to menstruation or postnatal bleeding. The base ruling is that martyrs of the battlefield are not washed, but there is an exception for those in a state of janabah? Actually the general rule is that the martyr is not washed, but here there is a question whether the obligation of ghusl had already been established or was prevented. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that martyrdom only prevents the obligation of ghusl from becoming due, but does not nullify an already existing obligation. Therefore, if the woman became pure (blood stopped) but had not yet performed ghusl, martyrdom does not waive the obligation, so she must be washed. The Sahibayn argue that death cancels any pending obligation of ghusl, and martyrdom prevents it from becoming obligatory anew. Therefore, no ghusl is required. The passage then mentions two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa regarding the case where she is martyred before the blood stops. In the first narration, since ghusl was never obligatory on her (as blood was still flowing), she is not washed. In the second narration, which is considered sahih (correct), she is washed because death causes the blood to stop, and at the moment of cessation, flowing blood (which was present before death) makes ghusl obligatory.

Key Terms
حائضہ (ha'idah): A woman experiencing menstruation (hayd).
نفاس (nifas): Postnatal bleeding after childbirth.
شہید (shahid): One who dies fighting in the path of Allah (fi sabilillah) and is granted the special status of martyrdom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) She should be washed, because martyrdom only prevents the obligation but does not nullify it.
C) She should be washed only if the blood stopped before martyrdom.
D) She should not be washed under any circumstance.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between martyrdom as a mani' (preventer) versus rafi' (nullifier) determines whether a martyred woman who had become pure from menses must be washed; the mufta bihi view requires washing.

Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf: Washing a Child Martyr
Discusses whether a child martyr is washed, with proofs from both sides.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi disagreement on washing a child martyr and identify the 'illa (effective cause) behind each position.

Core Idea
The child martyr: does the sword's purification apply where there are no sins?

This passage presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: should a child who dies as a martyr on the battlefield receive a ceremonial bath (ghusl) like any other deceased, or is he honored by not being washed, just like the adult martyrs of Uhud? The disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two main disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, hinges on how they understand the reason ('illa) behind the exceptional treatment of martyrs.

Guided Path
1
State the positions of both sides
Clearly articulate what Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn say about washing the child martyr.
Imam Abu Hanifa says wash the child; the Sahibayn say do not wash.
2
Identify the 'illa from the opposing side (Sahibayn)
The Sahibayn argue that the reason for not washing martyrs is to honor their suffering (mazlumiyat). Since the child suffers more, he is more deserving of this honor.
Mazlumiyat refers to the state of being wronged.
3
Identify the 'illa from Imam Abu Hanifa
Imam Abu Hanifa argues that the reason for not washing the martyrs of Uhud was that the sword purified them from sins. A child is sinless, so the sword cannot purify him; thus, he must be washed like any other deceased person.
Purification from sins is the key 'illa for Imam Abu Hanifa.
4
Analyze how each 'illa applies to the child
Determine whether the child embodies the 'illa according to each side. For the Sahibayn, the child has greater mazlumiyat. For Imam Abu Hanifa, the child has no sins, so the 'illa of purification is absent.
This highlights the difference in determining the effective cause.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Imam Abu Hanifa: the child martyr must be washed.
The text refers to Imam Abu Hanifa as 'Imam Sahib', indicating preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اور بچہ اگر شہید کر دیا گیا تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک اس کو نسل دیا جائے گا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک غسل نہ دیا جائے۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ شہید سے غسل کا ساقط ہونا اس لئے ہے تا کہ اس کی مظلومیت کا اثر باقی رہے۔ پس شہید کو نسل نہ دینا اس کے اکرام کے پیش نظر ہے اور بچہ کی مظلومیت زیادہ ہے لہذا بچہ اس کرامت کا زیادہ مستحق ہے۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ شہداء احد کے حق میں میں تلوار نسل سے کافی ہو گئی۔ کیونکہ تلوار گناہوں سے پاک کر دیتی ہے۔ یعنی شہداء احد کو نسل اس لئے نہیں دیا گیا کہ تلوار نے ان کو گناہوں سے پاک کر دیا ہے اور چونکہ بچہ پر کوئی گناہ نہیں ہے اس لئے بچہ شہداء احد کے معنی میں نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب شہدا ءواحد کے معنی میں نہ ہوا تو شہداء احد کی طرح بچہ سے غسل بھی ساقط نہ ہوگا بلکہ بچہ کونسل دیا جائے گا۔
And if a child is martyred, then according to Imam Sahib (Abu Hanifa), he should be given a bath, and according to the two companions, he should not be given a bath. The proof of the two companions is that the omission of ghusl for the martyr is so that the effect of his being wronged remains. Therefore, not washing the martyr is for his honor, and the child's being wronged is greater, so the child is more deserving of this honor. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa is that regarding the martyrs of Uhud, the sword sufficed in place of a bath, because the sword purifies from sins. That is, the martyrs of Uhud were not given a bath because the sword purified them from sins. And since the child has no sins, the child is not in the meaning of the martyrs of Uhud. And when he is not in the meaning of the martyrs of Uhud, then ghusl will not be omitted for him like the martyrs of Uhud; rather, the child will be given a bath.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a child who is martyred. The underlying principle is that martyrs of the battlefield (shuhada' al-ma'rakah) are not washed as a sign of honor, because their bloodshed purifies them. However, the Sahibayn argue that not washing is an honor that signifies the martyr's unjust suffering. Since a child's suffering is even greater and the child is blameless, the child is more deserving of this honor, so he should also not be washed. Imam Abu Hanifa counters by focusing on the reason for not washing the martyrs of Uhud: it was because the sword cleansed them of sins. Since a child has no sins, the sword cannot cleanse him of sins, so the analogy fails. Therefore, the child should be washed like an ordinary deceased person. This difference shows how the 'illa (effective cause) is determined: the Sahibayn see the 'illa as manifesting injustice/honor, while Imam Abu Hanifa sees it as purification from sins. The text implies that the mufta bihi position is that of Imam Abu Hanifa, as he is referred to as 'Imam Sahib' and the Sahibayn's view is mentioned as opposing.

Key Terms
شہید (shahid): One killed in the path of Allah, specifically in battle.
مظلومیت (mazlumiyat): The quality of being wronged or oppressed.
علت (illa): The effective cause or ratio legis that justifies a legal ruling in qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason Imam Abu Hanifa gives for washing a child martyr?
A) The child is innocent and does not need purification from sins.
B) The child's suffering is greater and thus he deserves more honor.
C) The child's parents may request a bath.
D) There is no specific proof; it is a general ruling.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the mufta bighi position is that a child martyr must be washed because the 'illa for not washing martyrs (purification from sins) does not apply to sinless children.

Ruling on Removing Items from the Martyr and Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Base ruling plus inter-madhhab dispute over removal of non-shroud items.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on removing items from the martyr and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using specific hadith and qiyas.

Core Idea
Martyr's clothes stay, but fur and weapons are removed — they are not of the shroud's type.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi position on handling the martyr's body. The basic principle: the martyr is not washed and his blood is not cleaned. However, certain items that do not belong to the shroud must be removed. This ruling leads to a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that nothing should be removed. We will examine the evidence from both sides and understand the Hanafi reliance on a specific hadith and qiyas.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling for the martyr
Identify the core Hanafi ruling: the martyr is not washed, his blood is not cleaned, and his clothes are not removed. However, additional items that are not of the same type as the shroud are to be removed.
This distinguishes between 'clothes' (which remain) and 'non-shroud items' (which are removed).
2
Identify which items are removed
List the specific items mentioned: fur garments (farw), quilted garments (hashw), weapons (silah), and socks (khuff). Understand why they are removed — they are not of the jins (type) of kafan.
These items are considered 'additional' and not part of the burial shroud.
3
Present the Shafi'i proof
State the Shafi'i position: nothing should be removed. Their proof is the general hadith 'Wrap them in their clothes' (Zammiluhum), which implies all clothing stays.
The Shafi'i argue that the command is general and does not specify any exceptions.
4
Present the Hanafi proof
Explain the Hanafi evidence: the specific hadith of Ibn Abbas where the Prophet ordered removal of iron (weapons) and leather (fur) from the martyrs of Uhud. Also the use of qiyas: since these items are not of the type of shroud, they must be removed.
When general and specific hadiths conflict, Hanafis resort to qiyas.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Summarize the practical difference: Hanafis remove non-shroud items; Shafi'is leave all items. This affects how the martyr is shrouded and buried.
The dispute has a clear practical outcome in funeral preparations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The martyr's blood should not be wiped off, and his clothes should not be removed. Additional items should be removed. And the martyr should not be washed, nor his blood cleaned, nor should his clothes be removed, due to the hadith that we have narrated. And there should be removed from him the fur garment, the quilted garment (with stuffing), the weapon, and the socks, because they are not of the type of shroud. And they may increase or decrease whatever they wish to complete the shroud according to the sunnah. Explanation: If on the martyr's body there is a leather garment, a fur coat, etc., or a garment stuffed with cotton, or a weapon, or socks, then they should be removed. This is the madhhab of the Hanafi scholars. Imam Shafi'i said that nothing should be removed from the martyr's body. The proof of Imam Shafi'i is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him) 'Zammiluhum' (wrap them up) etc., meaning: wrap the martyrs in their clothes. There is no detail in it about which garment to wrap and which to remove. Therefore, the general application of the hadith requires that no garment be removed from the martyr's body. Our proof is the hadith of Ibn Abbas who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) commanded regarding the slain of Uhud that the iron (weapons) and the leather (fur) be removed from them, and that they be buried with their blood and their clothes. Apparently, these two hadiths are contradictory. Therefore, we leave both and turn to qiyas. And the qiyas is that the fur, etc., should be removed, because these things are not of the type of shroud. If on the martyr's body there are less than the sunnah number of clothes, then they are increased to the sunnah number, and if there are more than the sunnah number, they are reduced to the sunnah number.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the basic ruling that the martyr is not washed, his blood is not cleaned, and his clothes are not removed. However, items that are not part of the normal shroud (kafan) such as fur coats, quilted garments, weapons, and socks should be removed. The author then presents the inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that nothing should be removed based on the general command 'wrap them in their clothes'. The Hanafi response is that there is a specific hadith from Ibn Abbas which differentiates, and when the two hadiths appear contradictory, the Hanafis resort to qiyas: the items in question are not of the same type as the shroud, so they should be removed. Furthermore, the text adds that the number of cloths used as a shroud should be adjusted to the sunnah number (usually three pieces for a man or five for a woman). If the martyr is wearing fewer, extra cloths are added; if more, the excess is removed. This ruling ensures that the martyr is buried with the proper shroud while preserving the special status of not removing all clothes.

Key Terms
کفن (kafan): The shroud used to wrap the deceased for burial.
فرو (farw): A fur garment or coat made of animal skin.
حشو (hashw): A garment stuffed with cotton or similar material (quilted).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Items not of the same type as the shroud, such as weapons and fur coats.
D) Only the blood should be cleaned, but clothes remain.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi madhhab rules that items not of shroud type must be removed from the martyr's body, while the blood and other clothes remain, based on the specific hadith of Ibn Abbas and qiyas.

Definition and Rulings of Irtithath for a Martyr
Defines irtithath and its exceptions for martyr washing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of irtithath, list the actions that constitute it, identify exceptions, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding wasiyya.

Core Idea
Irtithath: a wounded fighter loses the martyr's washing exemption if he seeks worldly comfort.

This passage from Al-Hidayah defines a critical legal concept: irtithath. When a fighter wounded in battle partakes in any worldly benefit before dying, he is no longer considered a martyr in the full sense – like the martyrs of Uhud – and must be given a full bath (ghusl). The text clarifies what actions count as worldly benefit, and highlights a subtle dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the effect of making a will for the Hereafter. Understanding irtithath is essential for correctly applying the rules of washing martyrs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
What is the hukm for a person with irtithath? The text says he must be washed (ghusl). Why? Because he is no longer in the meaning of the martyrs of Uhud.
The exception for not washing is only for those who truly match the condition of the Uhud martyrs.
2
List the actions that constitute irtithath
Eating, drinking, sleeping, receiving medical treatment, or being moved from the battlefield for comfort are explicitly mentioned. Obtain any 'marafiq al-hayah' – comforts of life.
These actions show the wounded person sought worldly ease, reducing the effect of oppression.
3
Identify the exceptions
Moving solely to avoid being trampled by horses does not count as irtithath. Also, if the person remains alive until the prayer time passes while conscious, that makes him 'murtath' because the prayer becomes a debt – a ruling of the living.
The key is intention: preservation vs. comfort.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on wasiyya
Abu Yusuf holds that making a will for matters of the Hereafter (e.g., charity, Hajj) is irtithath because it is a comfort (irtifaq). Muhammad says it is not, because such acts are from the rulings of the dead – not worldly benefit.
The text presents both views without explicit tarjih. The thamarah al-khilaf: if Abu Yusuf's view is taken, ghusl is wajib; if Muhammad's, it is omitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بھی ارسات حاصل کرنے والا ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ نماز اس کے ذمہ میں دین ہوگئی اور یہ زندوں کے احکام میں سے ہے۔ مصنف نے کہا کہ یہ امام ابو یوسف سے مروی ہے اور اگر امور آخرت میں سے کسی چیز کی وصیت کی تو ابو یوسف کے نزدیک یہ بھی ارتثاث ہوگا۔ کیونکہ یہ بھی راحت ہے۔ اور امام محمد کے نزدیک یہ ارشات نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہ مردوں کے احکام میں سے ہے۔ تشریح ارتبات کے معنی ہیں پرانا پڑ جانا۔ ثوب رث پرانے کپڑے کو کہتے ہیں۔ کچھ منافع حاصل کر لئے ہیں۔ حالانکہ شہداء احد کا حال یہ تھا کہ پانی ان کو پیش کیا جارہا ہے مگر انہوں نے نقصان شہادت کے خوف سے قبول نہ کیا اور یونہی تڑپ تڑپ کر جان دے دی۔ ہاں اگر کسی شہید کو مقتل سے اس لئے منتقل کیا گیا کہ مقتل میں اس کو گھوڑ ہے نہ روند ڈالیں ، تو یہ ار تثاث نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس نے کوئی راحت
Definition of Irtithath: And whoever experiences irtithath, he is given a bath. It is one who becomes old (in terms of martyrdom) in the ruling of martyrdom due to obtaining some benefits of life, because thereby the effect of oppression is lightened, so he is not in the meaning of the martyrs of Uhud. And irtithath is that he eats or drinks or sleeps or is treated or is moved from the battlefield, because he has obtained some benefits of life. And the martyrs of Uhud died thirsty while the cup of water was being passed around them, but they did not accept out of fear of diminishing their martyrdom. Except if he is carried from his place of death so that horses do not trample him, because he obtained no comfort. And if he is given shelter in a large or small tent, he is considered to have irtithath, due to what we have explained. And if he remains alive until the time of prayer passes while he is conscious, then he has irtithath, because that prayer becomes a debt upon him and that is from the rulings of the living. And the author said: This is narrated from Abu Yusuf. And if he makes a will concerning something of the matters of the Hereafter, then according to Abu Yusuf it is irtithath, because it is a means of comfort. And according to Muhammad, it is not irtithath, because it is from the rulings of the dead. Explanation: Irtithath means to become worn out. A worn-out garment is called 'thawb rath'. The gist of the issue is that if a person killed in the path of Allah obtains some benefits of life after being wounded and before dying, then it is said that this martyr has become 'old' (irtithath). And since due to obtaining benefits of life, the effect of oppression is lightened, he is no longer in the meaning of the martyrs of Uhud. And when he is not in the meaning of the martyrs of Uhud, he will be given a bath, because the omission of ghusl is only for the martyr who is in the meaning of the martyrs of Uhud. Sahib Quduri says that irtithath is that the one killed in the path of Allah eats something or drinks something or sleeps or receives medical treatment, or is moved from the battlefield for the purpose of comfort, because he has obtained some benefits of life. Whereas the condition of the martyrs of Uhud was that water was presented to them but they refused out of fear of diminishing their martyrdom, and they gave up their lives in agony. Yes, if a martyr is moved from the place of death so that horses do not trample him, then this is not irtithath, because he obtained no comfort.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage defines the legal concept of 'irtithath', which literally means becoming old or worn out, but in fiqh it refers to a wounded fighter who, before dying from his wounds, partakes in some worldly benefit. The text states that such a person loses the special status of a shahid who is not washed, and he must be given a full ghusl. The core underlying principle is that the martyr not being washed is a sign of honor that reflects the intensity of his oppression. The martyrs of Uhud exemplify this: they refused even water when offered because accepting it might diminish their reward. If a wounded person eats, drinks, sleeps, receives medical treatment, or is moved from the battlefield for comfort, he has availed himself of worldly benefits, thus reducing the efface of oppression. Therefore, he is no longer the same type of martyr and must be washed. The passage also gives two exceptions: moving solely to avoid being trampled by horses does not count as irtithath, because it is not for comfort. Additionally, if a wounded person stays alive until the time of prayer passes while conscious, that prayer becomes a debt (dayn) on him, which is a ruling for the living, so he is considered to have irtithath. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad over whether making a will regarding matters of the Hereafter (such as charity or pilgrimage) constitutes irtithath. Abu Yusuf says yes, because it is a form of comfort (irtifaq), while Muhammad says no, because such acts are from the rulings of the dead (i.e., they are recommended for everyone, not a sign of seeking worldly benefit).

Key Terms
ارتثاث (irtithath): A legal term meaning that a wounded fighter partakes in worldly benefits after being wounded but before dying, thereby losing the special status of martyrdom regarding being washed.
مرافق الحياة (marafiq al-hayah): The benefits or comforts of worldly life.
شهداء احد (shuhada' Uhud): The martyrs of the Battle of Uhud, who are the paradigmatic example of martyrs who were not washed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT considered irtithath according to the text?
A) Eating or drinking after being wounded.
B) Being moved from the battlefield so that horses do not trample you.
C) Sleeping after being wounded.
D) Receiving medical treatment.

Key Takeaway
Irtithath is a legal concept that removes the special washing exemption for a martyr when he obtains worldly comfort after being wounded; understanding it ensures correct application of ghusl rules.

intermediate
8 min
Irtithath: The Legal State That Nullifies Complete Martyrdom
Defines irtithath and lists actions that nullify martyrdom status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define irtithath, list the actions that constitute it, and apply the ruling to determine whether a wounded fighter is washed and prayed over.

Core Idea
Accepting any worldly benefit after being wounded strips the martyr of his special status — he must be washed.

This lesson explores the Hanafi concept of irtithath — a legal state that removes the complete martyrdom status from a fighter wounded in battle. The text from Al-Hidayah defines irtithath as obtaining any means of life before death, using the paradigmatic example of the martyrs of Uhud who refused water. We will examine the actions that trigger irtithath, the exceptions, and an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether afterlife-related wills count.

Guided Path
1
Define irtithath and its legal effect
Understand that irtithath (literally 'becoming worn out') is a legal classification: the wounded fighter who obtains worldly benefit before death loses the ruling of a complete martyr (no ghusl, no salat). He must be washed and prayed over.
The term derives from 'rath' meaning shabby clothing.
2
List the five actions that constitute irtithath
From the text: eating, drinking, sleeping, receiving medical treatment, or being moved from the battlefield for comfort. Each provides some means of life (marafiq al-hayat).
These are examples; any similar comfort likely applies.
3
Identify the exceptions
Moving a wounded fighter to avoid being trampled by horses does not constitute irtithath because no comfort is intended. However, sheltering him in a tent (even without intent for comfort) is irtithath because it grants protection.
The key is whether the action provides genuine comfort or relief.
4
Understand the proof from the martyrs of Uhud
The text cites the example: the martyrs of Uhud were offered water but refused it fearing deficiency in martyrdom. This proves that accepting worldly benefits reduces the status; conversely, refusing preserves it.
This is a dalil naqli (narrative evidence) with zanni thubut and dalala.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding wills for afterlife matters
Abu Yusuf considers making a will for charity or other afterlife matters as irtithath because it provides spiritual comfort. Muhammad argues it pertains to rulings of the dead and hence does not break martyrdom. The text does not settle, but later practice often follows Muhammad.
This ikhtilaf affects whether the body is washed or not.

ارتثاث کی تعریف
ومن ارتث غسل وهو من صار خلقا في حكم الشهادة لنيل مرافق الحيوة لان بذلك يخفف اثر الظلم فلم يكن في معنى شهداء احد والارتثاث ان يأكل أو يشرب أو ينام أو يداوى أو ينقل من المعركة لانه نال بعض مرافق الحياة، وشهداء احد ماتوا عطاشا والكأس تدار عليهم فلم يقبلوا خوفا من نقصان الشهادة الا اذا حمل من مصرعه كيلا تطأه الخيول لانه ما نال شيئا من الراحة ولو اواه فسطاط اوخيمة كان مرتثا لما بينا ولو بقى حيا حتى مضى وقت صلواة وهو يعقل فهو مرتث لان تلك الصلواة صارت دينا في ذمته وهو من احكام الأحياء وقال وهذا مروى عن ابى يوسف ولو اوصى بشئى من أمور الاخرة كان ارتثاثا عند ابي يوسف لانها ارتفاق و عند مـحـمـد لا يكون لانــــه مــن احـكـام الاموات

اس حال میں زندہ رہا کہ اسکے ہوش و حواس باقی ہیں تو یہ بھی ارتثاث پانے والا ہوگا۔ کیونکہ یہ نماز اس کے ذمہ میں دین ہو گئی اور نماز کا کسی کے ذمہ میں دین ہونا دنیا کے احکام میں سے ہے۔ صاحب ہدا یہ کہتے ہیں کہ یہ امام ابو یوسف کی روایت ہے اور اگر مقتول فی سبیل اللہ نے امر آخرت میں سے کسی چیز کی وصیت کی تو امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک یہ بھی ارتثاث ہے کیونکہ یہ حصول ثواب کی راحت ہے اور امام محمد کے نزدیک یہ ارمثاث نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ مردوں کے احکام میں سے ہے۔
Chapter of the Martyred. If the body of a martyr has fewer clothes than the recommended number, then increase them to the recommended number; if more than the recommended number, then reduce to keep the recommended number. Definition of Irtithath: And whoever becomes irtithath (old in the ruling of martyrdom) is washed, and he is the one who became worn out in the ruling of martyrdom due to obtaining the means of life, because thereby the effect of oppression is lightened, so he is not in the status of the martyrs of Uhud. And irtithath is: that he eats or drinks or sleeps or is treated medically or is moved from the battlefield, because he obtained some means of life. And the martyrs of Uhud died thirsty while the cup was passed around to them, but they did not accept out of fear of deficiency in martyrdom. Except if he was carried from his place of killing so that horses do not trample him, because he did not obtain any comfort. And if he was sheltered by a large tent or small tent, he becomes irtithath for what we have stated. And if he remained alive until the time of a prayer passed while he was rational, then he is irtithath, because that prayer became a debt in his obligation, and that is from the rulings of the living. And he said: This is narrated from Abu Yusuf. And if he bequeathed something from matters of the afterlife, then it is irtithath according to Abu Yusuf because it is a comfort, and according to Muhammad it is not irtithath because it is from the rulings of the dead. Translation: And a person who incurs irtithath is washed, and he is the one who became old in martyrdom due to obtaining the means of life. Because thereby the effect of oppression is lightened, so he is no longer in the status of the martyrs of Uhud. And irtithath is: that he eats or drinks or sleeps or is treated or is moved from the battlefield, because he obtained some means of life. And the martyrs of Uhud died thirsty while the cup of water was being passed around to them, but they did not accept out of fear of diminishing martyrdom. Except when he was lifted from the place of killing so that horses do not trample him, because he did not obtain any comfort. And if he was given shelter in a large or small tent, he has incurred irtithath, due to the evidence we stated. And if he remained alive until the time of a prayer passed while he has understanding, then he is one who incurred irtithath, because this prayer became a debt in his obligation, and this is from the rulings of the living. The author said that it is narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf. And if he left a will for anything from the matters of the afterlife, then according to Abu Yusuf this is also irtithath because it is comfort, and according to Imam Muhammad it is not irtithath because it is from the rulings of the dead. Explanation: The meaning of irtithath is to become old. Thaub rath means shabby clothing. The scenario is: If the one killed in the path of Allah obtains some means of life after being wounded and before dying, he is considered worn out in martyrdom. Because due to obtaining means of life, the effect of oppression is lightened. Therefore he is no longer in the status of the martyrs of Uhud. And when he is not in their status, he is washed, because the omission of washing applies only to the martyr who is in the status of the martyrs of Uhud. Sahib Quduri says: Irtithath is that the one killed in the path of Allah eats or drinks something before death, or sleeps, or receives medical treatment, or is moved from the battlefield for comfort, because he has obtained some means of life. While the state of the martyrs of Uhud was that water was presented to them but they did not accept it out of fear of diminishing martyrdom, and they gave up their lives in agony. Yes, if a martyr is moved from the place of killing so that horses do not trample him in the battlefield, then this is not irtithath, because he did not obtain any comfort. And if he was given shelter in a large or small tent, he will be considered one who incurred irtithath. And if a martyr remained alive until the time of one prayer passed and he remained alive with his senses intact, then he is also one who incurred irtithath, because this prayer became a debt in his obligation, and prayer becoming a debt on someone is from the rulings of the world. Sahib Hidayah says that this is a narration from Imam Abu Yusuf. And if the one killed in the path of Allah left a will for something from the matters of the afterlife, then according to Imam Abu Yusuf this is also irtithath because it is comfort in gaining reward, and according to Imam Muhammad it is not irtithath because it is from the rulings of the dead.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on the martyr (shahid). It defines the concept of irtithath — a legal state where a wounded fighter who, after receiving fatal injuries but before dying, derives any worldly benefit (eating, drinking, sleeping, medical treatment, or being moved from the battlefield for comfort) loses the special status of a complete martyr like the martyrs of Uhud. The ruling is that such a person must be washed (ghusl) and prayed over like an ordinary deceased Muslim, because the benefit lightens the effect of oppression. The proof is the example of the martyrs of Uhud who refused water despite extreme thirst, thereby preserving their complete martyrdom. Exceptions are made: moving to avoid being trampled by horses does not constitute irtithath, nor does being sheltered if no comfort was intended. The passage also includes an intra-Hanafi dispute: if the dying person makes a will for afterlife matters (e.g., charity), Abu Yusuf considers this irtithath because it provides spiritual comfort, while Muhammad does not, as it pertains to the rulings of the dead (which do not nullify martyrdom). The commentary clarifies that the core principle is whether any material or worldly comfort was received before death.

Key Terms
ارتثاث (irtithāth): The legal state of a wounded fighter who, after receiving injuries and before death, obtains any worldly benefit, thereby losing the full status of martyrdom.
شهيد (shahīd): One who is killed in the path of Allah and dies as a result; the ruling is that he is not washed nor prayed over, except in cases of irtithath.
شهداء احد (shuhadā' Uḥud): The martyrs of the Battle of Uhud who refused water and other comforts, serving as the paradigm of complete martyrdom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a wounded fighter in battle is moved from the battlefield to avoid being trampled by horses but does not receive any other comfort, what is the ruling?
A) He becomes irtithath and must be washed.
B) He does not become irtithath because he received no comfort.
C) He is still a complete martyr and not washed.
D) He is washed but still considered a martyr.

Key Takeaway
Irtithath, defined as obtaining any worldly comfort before death, nullifies the complete martyrdom status, requiring the deceased to be washed and prayed over.

Taharah — Purification

Ghusl for a Murdered Body: Diyah vs Qisas
Explanation of the Hanafi rule on ghusl for a slain body in a city based on whether diyah or qisas applies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl for a murdered body found in a city and distinguish when washing is obligatory versus omitted based on whether diyah or qisas is due.

Core Idea
A martyred victim of qisas is not washed because no worldly benefit reaches him, just like the martyrs of Uhud.

The Hanafi school has a detailed ruling on whether a murdered body found in a city should be washed. The key factor is the type of obligation arising from the killing: if diyah (blood money) is due, the deceased receives a benefit (e.g., debt payment), lightening the oppression, so he is washed like an ordinary person. If qisas (retaliation) is due, the deceased receives no worldly benefit, remaining a complete victim, and thus is not washed—earning the status of a shahid (martyr). This lesson unpacks that distinction and its proof by analogy to the martyrs of Uhud.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Ruling
State the default ruling: a murdered body found in a city is washed because qasamah and diyah are obligatory, providing benefit to the deceased.
Diyah benefits the deceased directly (e.g., paying debts), reducing the oppression.
2
Recognize the Exception
If it is known that the victim was killed unjustly with an iron weapon (e.g., sword), then qisas is obligatory, and the deceased is not washed—he is a martyr.
The weapon must be one that kills instantly, like a sword (per Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
3
Trace the Proof: Qiyas to Uhud
The proof is analogy to the martyrs of Uhud: they received no worldly benefit, so they were not washed. Similarly, in qisas cases, the deceased receives no benefit (the benefit goes to heirs and society), so washing is omitted.
The illa (effective cause) is absence of worldly benefit to the deceased.
4
Analyze the Key Objection and Response
An objection: the Uhud martyrs had no obligation at all, but qisas cases do have an obligation (qisas). The response: qisas benefits heirs and society, not the deceased himself, so the deceased is still without worldly benefit, preserving the analogy.
This strengthens the illa.
5
Note the Intra-Hanafi Nuance
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad specify that only weapons that kill instantly (like a sword) justify qisas. Slower weapons may still result in diyah.
This nuance affects whether the victim is washed or not.

شہر میں پائے جانے والے مقتول کے غسل کا حکم
ومن وجد قتيلا في المصر غسل لان الواجب فيه القسامة والدية فخفف اثر الظلم الا اذا اعلم انه قتل بحديدة ظلما لان الواجب فيه القصاص وهو عقوبة والقاتل لا يتخلص عنها ظاهرا اما في الدنيا واما في العقبي وعندابی یوسف و محمد مالا يلبث كالسيف ويعرف الجنايات ان شاء الله تعالى
ترجمہ اور جو شخص شہر کے اندر مقتول پایا گیا اس کو غسل دیا جائے کیونکہ اس قتل میں واجب تو قسامت اور دیت ہے۔ اس لئے ظلم کا اثر بلکا پڑ گیا۔ مگر جب یہ معلوم ہو کہ یہ دھار دار آلہ سے ظلماً قتل کیا گیا ہے۔ اسلئے کہ اس میں قصاص واجب ہے اور وہ عقوبت ہے اور قاتل بظاہر اس سے چھٹکارا نہ پاسکے گایا تو دنیا میں یا آخرت میں۔ اور امام ابو یوسف اور امام محمد کے نزدیک جو چیز دیر نہیں کرتی وہ تلوار ہے اور یہ مسئلہ باب الجنایات میں انشاء اللہ معلوم ہوگا۔
تشریح مسئلہ، اگر کوئی مقتول شہر کے اندر پایا گیا اور اس کا قاتل معلوم نہیں تو اس کو غسل دیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں اہل محلہ پر دیت واجب ہوگی اور اس دیت کا نفع میت کو پہنچے گا۔ چنانچہ مقتول اگر مدیون ہو تو اس سے اس کا دین ادا کیا جائے گا۔ بہر حال جب دیت کا نفع مقتول کو حاصل ہوا تو اس پر سے ظلم کا اثر ہلکا پڑ گیا۔ اور جب یہ مقتول کامل مظلوم نہ رہا تو شہداء احد کے معنی میں بھی نہیں ہوگا۔ اور شہداء احد کی طرح اس سے غسل ساقط نہ ہو گا۔ ہاں اگر یہ معلوم ہے کہ دھار دار آلہ سے مقتول ہوا اور اسکا قاتل بھی معلوم ہے تو اس کو غسل نہ دیا جائے۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں قصاص واجب ہے۔ اور قصاص عقوبت ہے نہ کہ عوض اور جب قصاص عقوبت ہے عوض نہیں ہے تو ظلم کا اثر بھی ہلکا نہ ہوگا بلکہ مقتول کامل مظلوم ہو گا ۔ اور جب مکمل مظلوم ہے تو شہداء احد کے معنی میں ہونے کی وجہ سے اس کو نسل بھی نہ دیا جائے گا۔ اور رہا قاتل تو وہ بچ نہیں سکے گا۔ اس لئے کہ اگر قاتل پر قابو پالیا گیا تو دنیا ہی میں اس سزا کو بھگتے گا۔ اور اگر قابو نہ ملا تو آخرت میں بھگتے گا۔ حاصل یہ کہ اگر قتل کی وجہ سے قاتل یا اولیا ء قاتل یا اس کے عاقلہ پر دیت جب ہوئی تو مقتول دنیا میں شہید نہیں ہوگا۔ عام مردوں کی طرح اس کو بھی غسل دیا جائے گا اور اگر قتل کی وجہ سے قصاص واجب ہوا تو مقتول شہید ہوگا اور اس کو نسل نہیں دیا جائے گا۔ اس جگہ ایک سوال ہو سکتا ہے وہ یہ کہ جس کے قتل کی وجہ سے قصاص واجب ہوا ہے وہ شخص شہداء احد کے معنی میں نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ شہداء احد کے قتل کی وجہ سے کوئی چیز واجب نہیں ہوئی تھی اور جو شخص شہداء احد کے معنی میں نہ ہو اس کو نسل دیا جاتا ہے۔ لہذا اس کو بھی غسل دیا جانا چاہئے جس کے قتل کی وجہ سے قصاص واجب ہوتا ہے۔ جواب قصاص کا فائدہ اولیاء مقتول اور جملہ انسانوں کو پہنچتا ہے۔ مقتول کو کوئی فائدہ نہیں پہنچتا۔ پس جس طرح شہداء احد کو کوئی نفع حاصل نہیں ہوا۔ اسی طرح اس کو بھی کوئی نفع حاصل نہیں ہوا۔
Ruling on washing a dead body found in a city: And whoever is found dead in the city is washed, because the obligation in it is qasamah (oath) and diyah (blood money), so the effect of oppression is lightened. Except if it is known that he was killed unjustly with an iron instrument, because the obligation in it is qisas (retaliation), which is a punishment, and the killer cannot escape it apparently, either in this world or the Hereafter. And according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, that which does not linger is like a sword, and this is known in the chapter of crimes, Allah willing. Translation: And a person who is found killed inside the city is washed because in this killing, the obligation is qasamah and diyah, so the effect of oppression is lightened. But when it is known that he was killed unjustly with a sharp instrument, then qisas is obligatory, and it is a punishment, and the killer apparently cannot escape it, either in this world or the Hereafter. And according to Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, that which does not tarry is the sword, and this matter will be known in the chapter of crimes, Allah willing. Explanation: If a dead body is found in the city and the killer is unknown, then he is washed. Because in this case, diyah is obligatory upon the residents of the locality, and the benefit of this diyah reaches the deceased. For example, if the deceased was in debt, his debt will be paid from it. In any case, when the benefit of diyah reaches the deceased, the effect of oppression on him is lightened. And when he is no longer a complete victim, he is not in the status of the martyrs of Uhud, and ghusl is not omitted for him like for the martyrs of Uhud. Yes, if it is known that he was killed with a sharp instrument and his killer is also known, then he is not washed. Because in this case, qisas is obligatory, and qisas is a punishment, not a compensation. And when qisas is a punishment and not compensation, the effect of oppression is not lightened; rather the deceased is a complete victim. And when he is a complete victim, he is in the status of the martyrs of Uhud, so he is not washed. As for the killer, he will not escape. Because if the killer is captured, he will undergo the punishment in this world, and if not captured, he will undergo it in the Hereafter. The conclusion: If due to the killing, diyah is payable by the killer or his heirs or his 'aqila, then the deceased will not be a martyr in this world; he will be washed like ordinary people. And if due to the killing, qisas is obligatory, then the deceased is a martyr and he is not washed. At this point a question may arise: The person for whose killing qisas is obligatory is not in the status of the martyrs of Uhud, because nothing was obligatory due to the killing of the martyrs of Uhud. And whoever is not in the status of the martyrs of Uhud is washed, so such a person should also be washed. Answer: The benefit of qisas reaches the heirs of the deceased and all mankind, but no benefit reaches the deceased himself. So just as the martyrs of Uhud did not receive any benefit, similarly this person also did not receive any benefit. Therefore, he is not washed.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding a dead body found in a city when the killer is unknown. The general rule is that such a body is washed like any ordinary Muslim because the obligation of qasamah (oaths) and diyah (blood money) upon the locality provides some material benefit to the deceased (e.g., paying off debts), thereby lightening the effect of oppression and removing the status of a complete martyr. However, if it is known that the victim was killed unjustly with a sharp iron weapon (such as a sword), then qisas (retaliation) becomes obligatory, which is a punishment and not a compensation. Since no benefit reaches the deceased himself, the oppression remains full, and he retains the status of a complete martyr, thus he is not washed. The commentary also addresses a potential objection: since the martyrs of Uhud had no qisas or diyah, why is the person killed with a weapon treated like them? The answer is that the benefit of qisas goes to the heirs and society, not to the deceased, so the deceased receives no worldly benefit, just like the Uhud martyrs. The passage also notes the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad that a weapon that kills instantly is like a sword in this ruling.

Key Terms
قسامة (qasāmah): Oaths taken by the people of the locality when a murdered body is found and the killer is unknown; it leads to diyah obligation.
دية (diyah): Blood money paid to the heirs of the victim; in this context, it provides benefit to the deceased (e.g., paying debts), thereby reducing the victim's status as a complete shahid.
قصاص (qiṣāṣ): Retaliation (eye for an eye); a punishment for the killer that does not benefit the deceased, thus the victim retains full martyr status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is a murdered person found in a city treated as a martyr (not washed)?
A) When his killer is unknown and diyah is due.
B) When he was killed with a sharp iron weapon and qisas is due.
C) When he was killed with a blunt weapon and diyah is due.
D) When he was killed during a battle, not in a city.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the determination of ghusl for a murdered body found in a city hinges on whether diyah (benefit to deceased) or qisas (no benefit) is due, with the proof being qiyas to the martyrs of Uhud.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Ruling on Washing and Praying over Those Executed in Hadd or Qisas
Establishes the ruling for washing and praying over those executed in hadd or qisas, with evidence from the hadith of Ma'iz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that those killed in hadd or qisas are washed and prayed over, identify the evidence from the hadith of Ma'iz, and explain why they are not considered shuhada.

Core Idea
Even criminals executed for hadd or qisas receive full burial rites; they are not martyrs but are still entitled to ghusl and janazah prayer.

This passage clarifies a crucial point in Islamic funeral rites: whether those executed for crimes (hadd) or under retributive justice (qisas) should be washed and prayed over. The Hanafi school, based on the Prophet's command regarding Ma'iz, affirms that they are, distinguishing them from the martyrs of Uhud who died purely for Allah's pleasure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is washing and praying over a deceased Muslim. The ruling is that it is fard kifayah, and it applies to those executed in hadd or qisas.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith of Ma'iz where the Prophet ordered his Companions to wash and pray over him after stoning.
The hadith is quoted in the explanation section.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit imperative 'wash him and pray over him' shows that hadd execution does not strip the deceased of burial rites.
4
Note the distinction from shuhada
The martyrs of Uhud are not washed because they died fighting for Allah's cause. Executed criminals die to fulfill a legal right, so they are not equated with shuhada.
5
Understand the intra-Hanafi nuance on qisas
There is a difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding whether qisas applies if a heavy object is used. The passage notes that if qisas is obligatory (Sahibayn's view), the victim may be considered a shahid and not washed, but the executed criminal is always washed.
This is a separate issue discussed in Kitab al-Jinayat.
